The Definition, Evidence, and Conditions of Iqrar (Acknowledgment)
Opening definition and proof of iqrar, with conditions and dispute on drunkenness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of iqrar, identify its conditions and pillars, and apply the Hanafi positions on the validity of iqrar from a drunken person.

Core Idea
Iqrar binds only the person who makes it; its validity requires capacity, free will, and verbal expression.

The Book of Iqrar follows the discussion of claims because a Muslim should acknowledge the truth rather than deny it. This opening defines iqrar, establishes its binding force from four sources, and sets forth its conditions and pillars. A key intra-Hanafi dispute concerns whether a drunk person's acknowledgment is valid, depending on the cause of intoxication.

Guided Path
1
Define iqrar linguistically and technically
Identify the linguistic root (qarra = established) and the technical definition given by the author: 'informing of a right binding on the informant.' Note that iqrar oscillates between truth and falsehood but truth is preponderant.
The technical definition makes iqrar a form of testimony against oneself.
2
Identify the four proofs for iqrar
From the text, extract the Quranic verses (2:282, 4:135), the Sunnah (cases of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah), the consensus of the ummah, and the rational argument (a sane person does not harm himself falsely).
These proofs establish iqrar as a binding legal act.
3
Distinguish iqrar from evidence (bayyinah)
Understand that iqrar is proof by itself, binding only on the person who makes it, whereas bayyinah requires a judge's ruling and binds all because the judge has general authority.
This distinction affects scope: iqrar does not affect others (e.g., children of a slave).
4
List the conditions and pillars of valid iqrar
From the text, extract the conditions: intellect, puberty, freedom (in some cases), and free will/consent. The pillar is verbal words (alfaz). Preventers (mawani) include compulsion, lack of intellect, and intoxication by permissible means.
The conditions are standard for legal capacity except freedom is context-dependent.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the drunk's iqrar
Note the split: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that iqrar from someone drunk by permissible means (compulsion, grain/honey drink) is not binding; Imam Muhammad holds it is binding. The text sides with the two (عندها). Also note the exception for hudood when drunk by forbidden means.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is that iqrar is void if intoxication was by permissible means.

هو إخبار
(هو إخبار )
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وأما الإجماع: فالأمة أجمعت على أنَّ الإقرار حجةٌ في حق نفسه، حتى أوجبوا عليه الحدود والقصاص بإقراره، وإن لم يكن حجّة في حق غيره ؛ لعدم دلالته عليه،
فالمال أولى.
وأما المعقولُ : فإنَّ العاقل لا يقرُّ على نفسه كاذباً ، بما فيه ضرر على نفسه أو ماله، فترجحت جهة الصدق في حق نفسه ؛ لعدم التهمة، وكمال الولاية، بخلاف
إقراره في حق غيره ، حتى لو أقر مجهول النسب بالرق جاز ذلك على نفسه وماله. ولا يصدق على أولاده وأُمَّهاتِهم ومدبّريه ، ومكاتبيه، بخلاف ما إذا ثبت بالبينة ؛ لأنَّ البينة إنما تصير حجَّةً بالقضاء، وللقاضي ولاية عامة فينفذ في حق الكل، أمّا الإقرار فحجة بنفسه، فلا يحتاج فيه إلى القضاء فينفذ عليه وحده لما ذكرنا ، إلا إذا ردَّه المقر له، فيرتد برده. كذا في «التبيين»(۱)، وغيره.
وأما شرطه : فالعقل والبلوغ بلا خلاف ، فلا يصح من المجنون والصبي إلا إذا كان الصبي مأذوناً له في التجارة، فيصح إقراره ؛ لأنَّ الصبي المأذون ملحق بالبالغ بحكم الإذن، وكذا المعتوه والمغمى عليه كالمجنون ؛ لعدم التمييز. وإقرار السكران جائز مطلقاً إذا كان سكره بطريق محظور ؛ لأنه ينافي الخطاب، إلا إذا أقر بما يقبل الرجوع كالحدود الخالصة لله ، وإن سكر بطريق مباح كالشرب مكرهاً لا يلزمه بشيء، وكذا بشرب المتخذ من الحبوب أو العسل عندها، خلافاً لمحمد
وأما الحرية فهي شرط في بعض الأشياء دون بعض، كما سيأتيك إن شاء الله
تعالى، وكذا الرضا والطوع شرط، فلا يصح إقرار المكره. وأما ركنه : فالألفاظ المذكورة فيما يجب به موجب الإقرار على المقر، صرح به في الرمز (۲)، و«النهاية» وغيرها.
[١]قوله : إخبار ؛ أي إعلام بالقول، فلو كتب أو أشار ولم يقل شيئاً لم يكن
The Book of Iqrar. Its relevance to what precedes is clear: the plaintiff either obtains what he claims or the defendant denies it, and it befits a Muslim to acknowledge the truth, just as it befits the plaintiff that his claim be true. Thus it is placed before what follows, which is conciliation. Iqrar is derived from 'qarra' meaning to establish, and 'aqarra ghayrahu' meaning to affirm it. In al-Misbah: 'Aqarra al-rajulu iqraaran: he was struck by cold, so he is maqroor irregularly; and aqarra bish-shay: he confessed to it.' End quote. Our teacher al-Tahtawi narrated from al-Hamawi: 'If it is sensory, one says aqarrahu; if verbal, one says aqarra bi hi. So iqrar is the establishment of what was wavering between denial and affirmation.' End quote. In Sharia: what the author explained by saying: 'It is an information about a right... etc.' and it is binding on the one making the acknowledgment and on what he acknowledged, because it indicates the existence of the informed matter; for iqrar oscillates between truth and falsehood, and the side of truth prevails because a person does not lie intentionally, since wealth is naturally beloved, and he would not speak falsely to another when he has complete intellect and religiosity. Its permissibility is established by the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and reason. As for the Book: His saying: 'And let the one on whom the right is due dictate' (2:282). In al-Madarik: 'The dictator is only the one upon whom the right is due, because he is the one attested to its establishment in his liability and his acknowledgment, so that is an acknowledgment against himself with his tongue, and imlal and imla' are two descriptions.' End quote. And His saying: 'Be persistently standing firm in justice, witnesses for Allah, even if against yourselves' (4:135). And what is meant by it is iqrar as they mentioned. As for the Sunnah: Verily the Messenger of Allah imposed stoning on Ma'iz by his confession, and on al-Ghamidiyyah by her confession, as reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim. So if a hadd punishment is established by a person's confession against himself, then wealth is more deserving to be established. As for consensus: The ummah has agreed that iqrar is proof against oneself, so much so that they made hudud and qisas obligatory by it, although it is not proof against others, because it does not indicate them. So wealth is more deserving. As for reason: A rational person does not confess falsely against himself regarding something harmful to himself or his wealth, so the aspect of truth is preponderant regarding himself, due to the absence of suspicion and the completeness of authority, contrary to his acknowledgment regarding others. Even if a person of unknown lineage confesses to being a slave, that is valid against himself and his property, but he is not believed regarding his children, their mothers, his mudabbars, and his mukatabs, contrary to when it is established by evidence, because evidence only becomes proof through judgment, and the judge has general authority so it is effective against all. As for iqrar, it is proof by itself, so it does not need judgment, and is effective only against himself, unless the person for whom it is made rejects it, then it is nullified by his rejection. Thus in al-Tabyin and others. As for its condition: Intellect and puberty without dispute, so it is not valid from a madman or a child, except if the child is permitted to engage in trade, then his acknowledgment is valid, because the permitted child is treated as an adult by virtue of permission. Likewise the insane and the unconscious are like the madman due to lack of discernment. The acknowledgment of a drunken person is valid absolutely if his drunkenness was by a forbidden means, because it nullifies sound speech, except if he acknowledges something that can be withdrawn, like hudood that are purely for Allah. And if he became drunk by a permissible means, like drinking under compulsion, nothing is binding on him, likewise drinking from grains or honey according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. As for freedom, it is a condition for some things and not others, as will come to you if Allah wills. Likewise, consent and willingness are conditions, so the acknowledgment of one under compulsion is not valid. As for its pillar: The words mentioned regarding what requires the cause of acknowledgment to be binding on the acknowledger, as stated in al-Ramz, al-Nihaya, and others. [1] His saying: 'Akhabar' means informing by speech, so if he wrote or gestured and did not say anything, it is not [valid].
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Iqrar (Acknowledgment) from the commentary on al-Wiqayah by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. It begins by explaining the placement of this chapter after the previous one, noting that it befits a Muslim to acknowledge the truth, so it precedes conciliation. The term iqrar is defined linguistically and technically: linguistically it means establishing or affirming, and technically it is an informing of a right, which is binding on the person making the acknowledgment. The passage then establishes the permissibility and binding nature of iqrar through four sources: Quran (2:282 and 4:135), Sunnah (cases of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah), consensus (the ummah's agreement that iqrar is proof against oneself), and rational argument (a sane person would not lie to his own detriment). It also discusses the scope of iqrar: it is proof only against the person himself, not against others, and it does not require a judge's ruling to be effective, unlike evidence (bayyinah) which requires judgment. The passage then details the conditions (shurut) for a valid iqrar: intellect and puberty (without dispute), and adds details about the madman, child, insane, and drunken person. For the drunk, the ruling depends on the means of intoxication: if by a forbidden means, his iqrar is valid except for matters that can be withdrawn like hudood; if by a permissible means (e.g., compulsion), it is not binding. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement here: Imam Muhammad differs from the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding drink from grains or honey. Further conditions include freedom (in some cases) and free will (consent, not compulsion). Finally, the pillar (rukn) of iqrar is the specific words uttered. The hashiya clarifies that 'ikhbar' means verbal informing, so gestures or writing are insufficient. This comprehensive opening sets the foundation for the entire book on acknowledgment.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Linguistically: establishing or affirming. Technically: informing of a right that is binding on the informant.
المقر (al-muqirr): The person who makes the acknowledgment.
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence requiring a judge's ruling to become proof; distinct from iqrar which is proof by itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling on the iqrar of a person who became drunk by drinking a beverage made from honey (a permissible means)?
A) His iqrar is valid and binding in all cases.
B) His iqrar is not binding.
C) His iqrar is valid only for property matters, not hudood.
D) His iqrar is valid only if he confirms it when sober.

Key Takeaway
Iqrar is a powerful proof against oneself, but its validity hinges on the muqirr having full legal capacity and free will; intoxication from permissible means void it.

Definition, Proofs, Conditions and Pillar of Iqrār (Acknowledgement)
Defines iqrār and provides proofs, conditions, and pillar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define iqrār according to the Hanafi school, state its proofs (ijma and rational), and list its conditions (sanity, puberty, volition, etc.) and pillar (verbal statement).

Core Idea
Iqrār is an informative statement binding on the declarant alone.

Iqrār (acknowledgment) is a key legal instrument in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson unpacks its definition as ikhbār, the consensus and rational proofs that make it binding, the required conditions for validity, and its single pillar. Understanding these elements is crucial for correctly applying iqrār in matters of debts, penalties, and personal status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Recognize that iqrār is defined as an informative statement (ikhbār) of a right due upon oneself. This distinguishes it from creative acts (inshā').
The passage opens with 'هو إخبار' – it is an informative statement.
2
Examine the proofs
The passage provides two proofs: consensus (ijma) that iqrār is binding on the declarant, and a rational argument that a sane person does not lie against their own interest.
Ijma is the primary proof; the rational argument reinforces it.
3
List the conditions
Identify the conditions required for a valid iqrār: sanity, puberty, volition, consent, and for some matters freedom. Note exceptions for permitted minors and certain intoxicated persons.
Conditions are given explicitly in the passage.
4
Identify the pillar
The pillar (rukn) of iqrār is the verbal statement (alphaẓ) that obligates the declarant. Gestures or writing without speech generally do not constitute iqrār, but writing to an absent person is considered like speech.
The passage states 'ركنه فالألفاظ المذكورة'.
5
Note exceptions and scope
Understand that iqrār binds only the declarant, not others. It is nullified by the recipient's rejection. Exceptions exist for minors permitted to trade and for intoxication depending on the cause.
The passage discusses the example of a person declaring himself a slave.

Source Text

هو إخبار
وأما الإجماع: فالأمة أجمعت على أنَّ الإقرار حجةٌ في حق نفسه، حتى أوجبوا عليه الحدود والقصاص بإقراره، وإن لم يكن حجّة في حق غيره ؛ لعدم دلالته عليه، فالمال أولى.
وأما المعقولُ : فإنَّ العاقل لا يقرُّ على نفسه كاذباً ، بما فيه ضرر على نفسه أو ماله، فترجحت جهة الصدق في حق نفسه ؛ لعدم التهمة، وكمال الولاية، بخلاف إقراره في حق غيره ، حتى لو أقر مجهول النسب بالرق جاز ذلك على نفسه وماله. ولا يصدق على أولاده وأُمَّهاتِهم ومدبّريه ، ومكاتبيه، بخلاف ما إذا ثبت بالبينة ؛ لأنَّ البينة إنما تصير حجَّةً بالقضاء، وللقاضي ولاية عامة فينفذ في حق الكل، أمّا الإقرار فحجة بنفسه، فلا يحتاج فيه إلى القضاء فينفذ عليه وحده لما ذكرنا ، إلا إذا ردَّه المقر له، فيرتد برده. كذا في «التبيين»، وغيره.
وأما شرطه : فالعقل والبلوغ بلا خلاف ، فلا يصح من المجنون والصبي إلا إذا كان الصبي مأذوناً له في التجارة، فيصح إقراره ؛ لأنَّ الصبي المأذون ملحق بالبالغ بحكم الإذن، وكذا المعتوه والمغمى عليه كالمجنون ؛ لعدم التمييز. وإقرار السكران جائز مطلقاً إذا كان سكره بطريق محظور ؛ لأنه ينافي الخطاب، إلا إذا أقر بما يقبل الرجوع كالحدود الخالصة لله ، وإن سكر بطريق مباح كالشرب مكرهاً لا يلزمه بشيء، وكذا بشرب المتخذ من الحبوب أو العسل عندها، خلافاً لمحمد
وأما الحرية فهي شرط في بعض الأشياء دون بعض، كما سيأتيك إن شاء الله تعالى، وكذا الرضا والطوع شرط، فلا يصح إقرار المكره. وأما ركنه : فالألفاظ المذكورة فيما يجب به موجب الإقرار على المقر، صرح به في الرمز، و«النهاية» وغيرها.
[١]قوله : إخبار ؛ أي إعلام بالقول، فلو كتب أو أشار ولم يقل شيئاً لم يكن إقراراً، ويدخل فيه ما إذا كتب إلى الغائب : أما بعد ؛ فله علي كذا، فإنه كالقول شرعاً. ذكره القهستاني نقلا عن «الصغرى». [١] قوله : بحق ؛ أي بما يثبت ويسقط من عين وغيره، ولكنه لا يستعمل إلا في حق المالية، فيخرج عنه ما دخل في حق التعزير ونحوه. كذا في «جامع الرموز» للقهستاني. [٢] قوله : لآخر عليه ؛ أي لغير المخبر على المخبر، وهذا احتراز عن الإنكار والدعوى والشهادة كما لا يخفى على المتدبر ، قال البرْجَندي: «وهذا يشكل بإقرار الوكيل بالخصومة، والوصي، فإنَّ إقرارهما بحق لآخر على الموكل واليتيم». انتهى. وفيه أن الوكيل والوصي نائبان مناب الموكل واليتيم، فدفع الإشكال، وظهر حقيقة الحال. [٣] قوله : وحكمه...الخ ؛ يعني أن حكم الإقرار ظهور الخبر به للمقر له عليه ؛ لإثبات المقربه له بهذا اللفظ ؛ ولذا قالوا : إنَّ المقر له إذا علم أنَّ المقر كاذب في إقراره ثم أخذه منه، لم يحل له ديانة، إلا أنه أخذه عن طيب نفسه، فإنه تمليك مبتدأ. وإنما لم يكتف بالإثبات عن النفي، وجميعها مبالغة في نحوردّ ما قال بعضُ المشايخ: إن الإقرار إنشاء، وإنما أطلق إشارة إلى أن تصديق المقر له لم يشترط ، وإن ارتد برده، ولو صدقه ثم ردَّه لم يصح الردّ ، ولو رده ثم أعاد إقراره صح الإقرار. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره.
It is an informative statement. As for consensus: the ummah agreed that iqrār is a proof against oneself, so they imposed penalties and retaliation by his iqrār, even though it is not a proof against others because it does not indicate upon them, so (establishing) property is more appropriate. As for the rational: a rational person does not testify against himself falsely, including something harmful to himself or his property, so the aspect of truth is preponderant in his own case due to lack of accusation and completeness of authority, unlike his iqrār regarding others. Even if a person of unknown lineage declares himself to be a slave, that is valid regarding himself and his property, and he is not believed regarding his children, their mothers, his mudabbars, and his mukatibs, unlike if it is proven by evidence; because evidence only becomes a proof by judicial decision, and the judge has general authority so it applies to all, whereas iqrār is a proof by itself, so it does not require a judicial decision and thus applies only to him for what we have mentioned, unless the recipient rejects it, then it is nullified by the rejection. Thus in al-Tabyīn and others. As for its condition: sanity and puberty without disagreement; so it is not valid from the insane or a minor unless the minor is permitted to trade, then his iqrār is valid because a permitted minor is treated like an adult by virtue of permission; similarly the imbecile and the unconscious are like the insane due to lack of discernment. The iqrār of an intoxicated person is absolutely valid if his intoxication is by a prohibited means because it conflicts with (proper) speech, except if he declares something that is revocable like purely divine punishments. If he becomes intoxicated by a permissible means, like drinking under compulsion, nothing is binding upon him; similarly by drinking from grains or honey according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad (al-Shaybani). Freedom is a condition for some things and not others, as will come to you if Allah wills; similarly, consent and volition are conditions, so the iqrār of a coerced person is not valid. As for its pillar: the aforementioned words by which the basis of iqrār is binding upon the declarant, as stated in al-Ramz, al-Nihāya and others. [1] His saying: 'an informative statement' means notification by speech; so if he wrote or gestured and said nothing, it is not iqrār, and it includes if he wrote to an absent person: 'to proceed, so-and-so is owed by me' then it is like speech legally. Al-Qahustānī mentioned it from al-Sughrā. [1] His saying: 'regarding a right' means something that is established and ceases from property and other things, but it is only used for financial rights, so it excludes what enters into the right of discretionary punishment and the like. Thus in Jāmi‘ al-Rumūz by al-Qahustānī. [2] His saying: 'due upon another' means upon someone other than the informant regarding the informant, and this is a precaution against denial, claim, and testimony as is not hidden from the thoughtful. Al-Barjandī said: 'This is problematic with the iqrār of the agent for litigation and the guardian, because their iqrār of a right due upon the principal and the orphan.' End. In response, the agent and guardian are deputies in place of the principal and orphan, so the issue is resolved and the reality is clear. [3] His saying: 'and its ruling...' means that the ruling of iqrār is the emergence of the information by it for the recipient against him, to establish what is declared by this wording; therefore they said: if the recipient knows that the declarant is lying in his iqrār and then takes it from him, it is not permissible for him religiously, except that he took it with his (the declarant's) good pleasure, then it is a new transfer of ownership. He did not suffice with affirmation over negation, and all of it is an exaggeration in refuting what some mashāyikh said: that iqrār is a creation, but it was stated to indicate that the affirmation of the recipient is not a condition, although it is nullified by his rejection; if he affirms and then rejects, the rejection is invalid; if he rejects and then repeats his iqrār, the iqrār is valid. Thus in Majma‘ al-Anhur and others.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the definition of iqrār (acknowledgment) and its legal framework. It begins by stating that iqrār is an informative statement (ikhbār), not a creative act. The proof from consensus is that the entire Muslim community has agreed that one's acknowledgment is binding against oneself, as evidenced by the application of hudud penalties and qisas based solely on the confessor's words, even though it does not bind others. This consensus establishes iqrār's authority as a proof against the declarant only. The rational proof follows: a sane person would not lie to their own detriment, so truth is presumed. This presumption is strongest against oneself due to the absence of suspicion (tuhma) and the completeness of authority (wilāya kāmila). In contrast, iqrār regarding others is not binding, as the declarant lacks authority over them. The text then discusses an example: if a person of unknown lineage declares himself a slave, it is valid concerning himself and his property, but does not extend to children, mothers, mudabbars (slaves promised freedom upon owner's death), or mukatibs (slaves under contract of manumission). However, if the same fact is proven by evidence (bayyina), it applies to all, because evidence becomes a proof only through a judge's ruling, and the judge has general authority. iqrār, being a proof by itself, does not require a judicial decision and only affects the declarant, unless the recipient rejects it, in which case it is nullified. Next, the conditions (shurūt) of valid iqrār are explained: sanity (ʿaql) and puberty (bulūgh) are required without disagreement. iqrār from an insane person or a minor is invalid, except a minor permitted to trade, as they are treated like an adult in commercial matters due to permission. The imbecile (maʿtūh) and the unconscious (mugḥmā ʿalayh) are like the insane due to lack of discernment. The iqrār of an intoxicated person is valid if the intoxication was through a prohibited means (like wine), because it conflicts with rational speech; however, if he acknowledges something that is revocable, such as pure divine punishments (ḥudūd khāliṣa li-llāh), it is still valid. But if intoxication was through a permissible means, such as drinking under compulsion, or drinking from grains or honey (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees), his iqrār is not binding. Freedom (ḥurriyya) is a condition for some matters but not all, as will be explained later. Consent (riḍā) and volition (ṭawʿ) are conditions, so a coerced person's iqrār is not valid. Finally, the pillar (rukn) of iqrār is the specific words that obligate the declarant, as mentioned in al-Ramz and al-Nihāya. The commentary then elaborates on the definition phrase by phrase: 'ikhbār' means verbal notification; writing to an absent person is considered like speech legally. 'Bi-ḥaqq' (regarding a right) restricts iqrār to financial rights, excluding disciplinary penalties and the like. 'Li-ākhar ʿalayh' (due upon another) excludes denial, claim, and testimony, although an agent or guardian's iqrār is effective because they stand in place of the principal. 'Wa ḥukmuhu' (its ruling) means the effect is the emergence of the declared right for the recipient; if the recipient knowingly takes a false declaration, it is not permissible religiously (diyāna) unless taken willingly, which is a new gift. The mention of 'zuhūr al-khabar' (emergence of the information) indicates that affirmation by the recipient is not a condition for the iqrār to be effective legally, though it can be nullified by rejection.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): An informative statement (ikhbār) by which a person declares a right due upon himself to another.
إخبار (ikhbār): An informative statement reporting something already established, as opposed to creating it.
إجماع (ijmā‘): Consensus of the mujtahidūn of the ummah, here used as a proof for the binding nature of iqrār against oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition (shart) for the validity of iqrār?
A) The presence of a judge
B) The recipient's acceptance
C) Sanity and puberty
D) Two witnesses

Key Takeaway
Iqrār is an informative statement that, when conditions are met, is binding only on the declarant based on consensus and rational proof.

Iqrār as Ikhbār vs Inshā': Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Its Consequence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nature of iqrār and its procedural effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions on the nature of iqrār and identify the practical consequence (thamarah) that depends on this dispute.

Core Idea
Iqrār is ikhbār, not inshā'; therefore claims based on it are not heard.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute among later mashāyikh: is iqrār (confession/acknowledgment) an informative statement (ikhbār) or a creative act (inshā')? The answer affects whether a judge will entertain a lawsuit concerning the acknowledged right. The text from al-Ḥamawī declares the correct view as ikhbār and states the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Understand the key difference between ikhbār (reporting a pre-existing fact) and inshā' (creating a new legal effect) as applied to iqrār.
2
Recognize the correct view
Identify that the text declares the correct and muftā bihī view as ikhbār.
The text does not provide the detailed evidence but references lengthy works.
3
Trace the thamarah
Connect the dispute to the practical consequence: if iqrār is ikhbār, the right is considered established from the moment of iqrār, so a subsequent claim (daʿwā) is not heard; if it were inshā', the claim would be required to enforce the newly created right.
4
Understand the tarjih
Note that the fatwa-giving opinion is the ikhbār view, and that it is considered 'asahh (more sound) among the later Hanafi scholars.
The tarjih is based on the predominant understanding of iqrār in Hanafi usul.
5
Apply to a scenario
Apply the rule: a person acknowledges a debt (iqrār) in court. Under the ikhbār view, the judge does not hear a separate claim because the debt is already established. Consider how this differs if the acknowledgment were inshā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العلامة الحموي في «غمز عيون البصائر»: «واختلف مشايخنا في الإقرار هل هو إخبار أو إنشاء ، والصحيح الأوّل، والاستدلال لكل من القولين مبسوط في المطولات.
واعلم أنه يبتني على الاختلاف المذكور سماعُ دعوى الأموال والأعيان، بناءً على الإقرار وعدمه، فمن قال بأنه إخبار ، قال : لا تسمع، هو الصحيح المفتى به ، كما
Allāma al-Ḥamawī said in Ghumz ‘Uyūn al-Baṣā’ir: 'Our mashāyikh differed on whether iqrār is ikhbār (informative) or inshā’ (creative), and the correct is the first. The evidence for each of the two views is detailed in lengthy works. Know that hearing of claims regarding properties and goods is based on this difference, depending on iqrār or its absence. Whoever said it is ikhbār says: claims are not heard; this is the correct and fatwa-given view, as...'
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute among later mashāyikh regarding the legal nature of iqrār: is it ikhbār (an informative statement, reporting a pre-existing right) or inshā’ (a creative statement that brings the right into existence)? The correct view according to the text is that iqrār is ikhbār. The practical consequence (thamarah) of this disagreement concerns whether a claim (daʿwā) based on an iqrār can be heard by a judge. According to those who hold iqrār is ikhbār, claims based on it are not heard, because the iqrār itself already establishes the right without needing a judicial process. This is the position that is considered correct and relied upon for fatwa. The passage ends mid-sentence; the complete discussion would continue to contrast the inshā’ view, which would allow hearing claims.

Key Terms
إخبار (ikhbār): An informative statement that reports a pre-existing fact, as opposed to creating a new legal effect.
إنشاء (inshā’): A creative statement that brings a legal effect into existence, like a contract.
سماع دعوى (samāʿ ad-daʿwā): The court's acceptance of a claim for hearing; i.e., whether a judge will entertain a lawsuit concerning a right that has been acknowledged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical consequence (thamarah) of the dispute over whether iqrār is ikhbār or inshā'?
A) It affects whether the recipient must accept the iqrār.
B) It determines whether a claim (daʿwā) based on the iqrār can be heard by a judge.
C) It changes the number of witnesses required.
D) It only affects the validity of iqrār in criminal cases.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, iqrār is considered an informative statement (ikhbār); thus, claims based on an acknowledgment are not heard because the right is already established.

Nature of Iqrār: Ikhbār vs Inshā'
Analyzing the legal nature of iqrār as ikhbār or inshā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that iqrār is a report (ikhbār) and not a creation (inshā'), and evaluate the practical consequences of this classification.

Core Idea
Iqrār manifests a pre-existing right, it does not create one — a subtle distinction with weighty legal results.

At first glance, the question of whether a confession (iqrār) is a report or a creative act might seem academic. However, this classification determines whether a claim based on iqrār is heard in court, whether a Muslim can confess to having wine, and whether an iqrār given under duress is valid. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi debate and the evidence that settles it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute's core
The central question: Is iqrār a report (ikhbār) of a pre-existing right, or does it create (inshā') a new right? Determine what each side claims.
2
Examine the proof from iqrār of wine
In the text, iqrār of wine for a Muslim is valid. Since a Muslim cannot own wine, if iqrār were creative, this would be impossible. Hence iqrār must be a report.
This shows that iqrār only manifests an existing state, not ownership.
3
Examine the proof from iqrār under duress
Iqrār of divorce or emancipation under duress is invalid. Duress indicates falsehood, and a report can be false. If iqrār were a creation, it would be valid because creation cannot be falsified.
This distinguishes iqrār from inshā' acts like divorce where duress does not nullify.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: If iqrār is a report, claims based solely on iqrār are not heard in court (only in defense). If it were creation, claims would be heard. Also, iqrār of wine valid only as report.
The fatwa follows the report view.
5
Note the fatwa hierarchy
The correct view is ikhbār, as held by al-Hamawi and the author. The minority view (inshā') is acknowledged but not relied upon for fatwa.
Mufta bihi: iqrār is a report.

The Book of Iqrār: Its Definition and Rulings: concerning a right of another upon him, and its ruling is the manifestation of the confessed matter, not its creation. [2] (with a right) of another upon him, and its ruling is the manifestation of the confessed matter, not its creation. It includes when one writes to an absent person: 'To proceed: so-and-so is owed by me such-and-such,' for it is like speech legally. Al-Qahistani mentioned it, quoting from 'al-Sughra'. [1] His saying: 'with a right' means that which is established and falls from a specific thing or other, but it is only used in matters of wealth, so it excludes what falls under the right of ta'zir and the like. Thus in 'Jami' al-Rumuz' by al-Qahistani. [2] His saying: 'of another upon him' means for other than the informant upon the informant, and this is a precaution against denial, claim, and testimony, as is not hidden from the thoughtful. Al-Burjandi said: 'This is problematic with the iqrār of the agent for litigation and the executor, for their iqrār of a right of another upon the principal and the orphan.' End. In it, that the agent and executor are deputies in place of the principal and orphan, so the problem is removed and the reality of the situation becomes clear. [3] His saying: 'and its ruling...etc.' means that the ruling of iqrār is the manifestation of the news to the confesse upon him; for proving the confessed matter to him by this wording. Hence they said: If the confesse knows that the confessor is lying in his iqrār, then takes it from him, it is not permissible for him religiously, unless he takes it willingly, then it is a new ownership. And he did not suffice with affirmation over negation, all of which is exaggeration in refuting what some shaykhs said: that iqrār is creation. And he only mentioned it indicating that the confirmation of the confesse is not required, and even if he rejects it, and if he affirms then rejects, the rejection is not valid, and if he rejects then repeats his iqrār, the iqrār is valid. Thus in 'Majma' al-Anhur' and others. Al-Hamawi said in 'Ghamz 'Uyun al-Basa'ir': 'Our shaykhs differed concerning iqrār: is it a report or a creation? The correct is the first, and the evidence for each of the two views is detailed in the lengthy works. Know that upon the mentioned difference depends the hearing of claims of wealth and properties, based on iqrār and its absence. So whoever said it is a report said: it is not heard; this is the correct and fatwa-worthy view, as in 'Jami' al-Rumuz' (2:250), 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:289). [PAGE_234] Zubdat al-Nihayah li'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: So iqrār of wine for a Muslim is valid, but not iqrār of divorce or emancipation under duress, and if a free person confesses, iqrār of wine for a Muslim is valid, not iqrār of divorce or emancipation under duress, because the ruling of iqrār is manifestation, not creation. Iqrār of wine for a Muslim is valid, but ownership of wine for him is not valid, and iqrār of divorce and emancipation under duress is not valid. If it were creation, it would be valid, because divorce and emancipation of a coerced person are effective in our school. And whoever said it is creation said: it is heard. As for the mentioned claim based on iqrār in the defense side, it is heard according to the fatwa, as recorded in 'al-Bazzaziyya'. Then know that if iqrār were a report as the author said, it would be appropriate that if someone confesses something and attributes it to a state of soundness, it would be from all of the property, but a contrary view will come in the next folio. End. [11] His saying: 'so it is valid...etc.' is a branch of the fact that the ruling of iqrār is manifestation of the confessed matter, not its creation. So if iqrār were creation, iqrār of wine for a Muslim would not be valid because a Muslim cannot validly own wine. In 'al-Muhit': 'If he confesses wine for a Muslim, it is valid and he is ordered to hand it over if he requests its return. If he confesses consumed wine for a Muslim, it is not valid because a Muslim is not owed compensation for wine.' End. [2] His saying: 'and iqrār of divorce and emancipation under duress is not valid' because evidence of falsehood exists, which is duress, and iqrār is a report that may be true or false, so it is permissible for the literal meaning to be contradicted. If it were creation, it would be valid because in creation, it is impossible for the literal meaning to be contradicted. [3] His saying: 'free' is a condition of freedom for his iqrār to be valid absolutely. For the restricted slave, his iqrār by tongue is postponed until after emancipation, similarly the authorized slave in matters not of trade, such as dower by intercourse with a woman he married without his master's permission, and crimes requiring compensation, because the permission only includes trade, so he is not authorized over it.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of iqrār (confession/acknowledgment). The text first defines iqrār as the informing of a right of another upon the confessor, with the ruling that it manifests (uzhur) the confessed matter, not creates it. The commentary then explains the key phrases: 'bi-haqq' means a right that can be established and removed, usually in financial matters; 'li-ākhar 'alayhi' excludes denial, claim, and testimony. An objection by al-Burjandi regarding the agent and executor is resolved by noting they are deputies. The main dispute (is it ikhbār or inshā') is introduced: some shaykhs said it is inshā', but the correct view is ikhbār. The practical consequence: if iqrār is a report, claims based on it are not heard; if it is creation, they are heard. The fatwa is on the first view. Then, applications: iqrār of wine for a Muslim is valid because it is merely reporting an existing fact; but ownership of wine cannot be transferred, so if the wine is consumed, the iqrār is invalid. Iqrār of divorce or emancipation under duress is invalid because duress indicates falsehood, and a report can be false; but if it were creation, it would be valid because creation cannot be contradicted. Finally, a condition: a slave's iqrār is not valid until he is free, and an authorized slave's iqrār in non-commercial matters is also invalid because his permission is limited to trade.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Informing by a person of a right of another upon him.
إخبار (ikhbār): A report or declaration that can be true or false; iqrār is considered a type of ikhbār.
إنشاء (inshā'): Creation or origination of a legal effect through speech; the opposing view holds iqrār is inshā'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is iqrār of wine for a Muslim valid even though a Muslim cannot own wine?
A) Because iqrār creates ownership, and the Muslim can own wine
B) Because iqrār is a report, and it only manifests an existing fact, not ownership
C) Because wine is not a property, so no ruling applies
D) Because the confessor is not required to deliver the wine

Key Takeaway
Iqrār is a report of a pre-existing right, not a creative act; this determines its validity under duress and with prohibited items.

The Nature of Iqrar: Why Acknowledging Wine is Valid but Acknowledging Divorce Under Duress is Not
Ruling on iqrar validity: wine vs duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that iqrar (acknowledgment) is ikhbar (informative) rather than insha (creative), and apply this principle to determine the validity of iqrar of khamr and iqrar of talaq/itq under duress.

Core Idea
Iqrar reveals an existing fact; duress breaks truthfulness, so iqrar under duress fails, but iqrar of non-ownable items like wine remains valid.

A common legal puzzle: why can a Muslim validly acknowledge owning wine, even though he cannot own it? And why does a forced acknowledgment of divorce count for nothing? The answer lies in the Hanafi classification of iqrar as an informative statement (ikhbar) rather than a creative one (insha). This lesson unpacks that distinction and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states two rulings: (1) Iqrar of wine (khamr) by a Muslim is valid (sahih). (2) Iqrar of divorce (talaq) or emancipation (itq) under duress is invalid. Note the contrast: one is valid despite the Muslim's inability to own wine; the other is invalid despite the fact that the actual divorce under duress is valid.
The actual talaq under duress is valid in Hanafi fiqh (waqi'), but its acknowledgment is not.
2
Understand the underlying principle: iqrar = ikhbar
The author explains: 'the ruling of iqrar is manifestation (zuhur) not creation (insha).' Iqrar is like testimony: it discloses an existing right or fact. It does not create a new legal consequence. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the function of iqrar.
If iqrar were insha, it would create the right from the moment of utterance, which would mean one could create ownership of wine (impossible) or divorce under duress would be created (but then why would it be invalid?).
3
Apply the principle to wine (khamr)
If iqrar were insha, acknowledging wine for a Muslim would be invalid because a Muslim cannot own wine. But the school holds it valid. Why? Because iqrar is ikhbar: it merely reports the pre-existing fact that wine is in his possession. The legal consequence of ownership does not follow.
This proves that iqrar cannot be insha, as the opposing view would have to deem iqrar of wine invalid, which contradicts the madhhab.
4
Apply the principle to duress
Iqrar is informative, so it carries a truth condition. Duress (ikrah) creates a presumption of falsehood: the person may have spoken under compulsion, not truthfully. Therefore, iqrar under duress is invalid. If iqrar were insha, duress would not affect it (since insha creates regardless of intent).
The actual talaq under duress is valid in Hanafi fiqh (because talaq is insha), but iqrar of that talaq is not, because iqrar is ikhbar.
5
Note the dispute and the slave's iqrar
The text mentions that some scholars (referred to as 'man qala innahu insha') hold iqrar to be insha. The Hanafi rebuttal uses the khamr case. Additionally, the passage addresses the condition of freedom: a slave's iqrar is generally deferred, except for hudud and qisas where he is treated as free. A minority (Ahmad, Zufar, etc.) holds the slave's iqrar for hudud valid.
This dispute shows the practical implications (thamarah) of the ikhbar vs insha classification.

Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on al-Wiqayah, dealing with the legal nature of iqrar (acknowledgment). The author establishes that iqrar is an informative statement (ikhbar), not a performative one (insha). The proof is rational: iqrar reveals an existing fact; it does not create a new legal consequence. Thus, iqrar of wine for a Muslim is valid because it merely discloses the existence of wine in his possession, even though he cannot own it. However, iqrar of divorce or emancipation under duress is invalid because duress indicates possible falsehood, and since iqrar is ikhbar, it must be truthful. In contrast, if iqrar were insha (like the actual pronouncement of divorce), the effect would occur regardless of duress. The passage also addresses the condition of freedom: a slave's iqrar is deferred until after emancipation, except in matters of trade if permitted. Regarding hudud and qisas, the slave is treated as free, so his iqrar does not affect his master; but there is a minority view (from Ahmad, Zufar, etc.) that it does.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right against oneself; in Hanafi fiqh, considered informative (ikhbar) not creative (insha).
إكراه (ikrah): Duress or coercion that overcomes a person's will.
خمر (khamr): Wine or any intoxicating drink made from grapes.
ظهور (zuhur): Manifestation or disclosure; the function of iqrar according to Hanafi fiqh.
إنشاء (insha): Creative performative statement that brings a legal consequence into existence, e.g., talaq, itq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the iqrar (acknowledgment) of wine (khamr) for a Muslim valid in Hanafi fiqh, whereas iqrar of divorce or emancipation under duress is not?
A) Because wine is not owned by the Muslim, so iqrar is meaningless
B) Because iqrar is considered ikhbar (information), not insha (creation), so it merely reveals an existing fact; duress indicates possible falsehood
C) Because divorce and emancipation under duress are invalid in themselves, so their acknowledgment is also invalid
D) Because wine is a permissible object for iqrar, while talaq and itq are not

Key Takeaway
Iqrar is ikhbar, not insha: this explains why acknowledging wine is valid (reveals possession) but acknowledging talaq under duress is invalid (duress defeats truthfulness).

Condition of Mukallaf for Valid Iqrar
Condition of mukallaf with exception for trade-permitted minors/idiots

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who can validly make an iqrar in Hanafi fiqh and identify the exception for discerning children and idiots permitted in trade.

Core Idea
An iqrar by a non-mukallaf is invalid, but a discerning child or idiot may validly acknowledge trade-related debts if permitted.

This lesson unpacks the first condition of a valid iqrar: the muqir (acknowledger) must be mukallaf – a legally responsible person. However, Hanafi law provides a practical exception for trade-related matters, allowing a discerning child or idiot to make valid acknowledgments if they have been granted permission to engage in trade. Understanding this exception is crucial for practitioners dealing with commercial transactions involving minors or those with limited capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The text establishes that the muqir must be mukallaf. In Hanafi fiqh, mukallaf means an adult, sane Muslim who is legally responsible.
The term 'mukallaf' is derived from taklif (legal obligation).
2
Note the general rule
Iqrar of a madman (majnun), idiot (ma'tuh), and discerning child (sabi 'aqil) is not valid because they lack the capacity for commitment (ahliyyat al-iltizam).
Capacity for commitment is the ability to undertake obligations.
3
Examine the exception
If a child or idiot is authorized (ma'dhun) in trade, their iqrar in trade-related matters (debt, deposit, loan, partnership, usurpation) is valid. This is because authorization implies capacity to acknowledge trade necessities.
The exception covers only trade necessities, not non-trade matters like dower or tort liability.
4
Test the boundaries
The iqrar of an authorized child/idiot is valid only for trade necessities, not for matters like dower (mahr), tort liability (jinaya), or guaranty (kafala). Why? Because those are not within the scope of trade permission.
The text specifically lists: kf al-dayn, al-wadi'a, al-ariya, al-mudaraba, al-ghasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٢) قوله : معلوم أو مجهول ؛ أما الأول فكما إذا قال له : علي ألف درهم أو مئة دينار، أو عمل ثوب، أو مثل ذلك ، وأما الثاني فكما إذا قال له : علي شيء أو حق. [٣] قوله : صح؛ أي إقراره، أما في إقراره بحق معلوم فظاهر، وأما في إقراره بحق مجهول فإن جهالة المقربه لا يمنع صحة الإقرار ؛ لأن الحق قد يلزمه مجهولاً، بأن أتلف مالاً لا يدري قيمته، أو جرح جراحة لا يدري أرشها، أو يبقى عليه بقية حساب لا يعرف قدرها، وهو محتاج لإبراء ذمته بالإيفاء أو بالتراضي. بخلاف الجهالة في المقر له، سواء تفاحشت بأن قال : علي ألف درهم لواحد من الناس، أو لم تتفاحش بأن قال : علي ألف درهم لأحد هذين ؛ لأن المجهول لا يصلح مستحقاً ، ا، إذ لا يمكن الجبر على البيان من غير تعيين المدعي، فلا يفيد فائدته. قال الزيلعي (٢) : هكذا ذكره شمس الأئمة له، وذكر شيخ الإسلام له في «مبسوطه»، والناطفي في «واقعاته»: إنها إذا تفاحشت لا يجوز، وإن لم تتفاحش جاز ؛ لأنَّ صاحب الحق لا يعدو من ذكره، وفي مثله يؤمر بالتذكّر ؛ لأنَّ المقر قد نسي صاحبَ الحق.
Book of Iqrar: Its Definition and Rulings. A legally responsible person (mukallaf) [acknowledging] a known or unknown right is valid. ... Except when acknowledging hudud and qisas, the slave is kept on the principle of freedom regarding them... [Commentary] The word 'mukallaf' is a condition because the iqrar of a madman, an idiot, and a discerning child is not valid due to lack of capacity for commitment... If the child or idiot is permitted in trade, his iqrar in trade-related matters is valid... The word 'known or unknown' – the first is like saying 'I owe a thousand dirhams' etc.; the second is like saying 'I owe something' or 'a right'... The validity of iqrar of an unknown right on the part of the acknowledging person is because he may be liable for an unknown amount... Unlike ignorance regarding the acknowledgee, whether excessive as saying 'I owe a thousand dirhams to one of the people' or not excessive as saying 'to one of these two' – because the unknown cannot be a lawful claimant... Al-Zayla'i said: Shams al-A'immah mentioned this, and Shaykh al-Islam in his Mabsut and al-Natafi in his Waqi'at said: if the ignorance is excessive, it is not permissible; if not excessive, it is permissible, because the right-holder is limited to those mentioned.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage introduces the book on iqrar (acknowledgment) by defining its essential conditions. The muqir (acknowledger) must be mukallaf (sane, adult). However, a discerning child or idiot who is permitted to trade can validly acknowledge matters necessary for trade. The object of acknowledgment (maqirr bihi) can be known or unknown on the part of the muqir; for example, if he owes a specific amount or an unspecified debt. This is because a person may legitimately owe an unknown amount. But the acknowledgee (maqirr lahu) must be identifiable; if he is totally unknown (e.g., 'to one of the people'), the iqrar is invalid because the claimant cannot be determined. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi holds that any vagueness regarding the acknowledgee invalidates the iqrar. Shaykh al-Islam al-Hilwani and al-Natafi distinguish: if the vagueness is excessive (e.g., 'to one of all people'), it is invalid; if mild (e.g., 'to one of these two'), it is valid, and the muqir should be asked to recall.

Key Terms
مكلّف (mukallaf): A legally responsible person: sane, adult Muslim
مأذون (ma'dhun): A person (child or idiot) who has been given permission by their guardian to engage in trade

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid iqrar according to the Hanafi position in this text?
A) A sane adult acknowledges a debt.
B) A 10-year-old child permitted in trade acknowledges a debt from selling goods.
C) A madman acknowledges a debt.
D) An idiot permitted in trade acknowledges a deposit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the condition of mukallaf for iqrar is relaxed for discerning children and idiots authorized in trade, but only for trade-related matters.

Known and Unknown Rights in Iqrar
Conditions regarding known and unknown rights in iqrar with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an iqrar of an unknown right (maqirr bihi) is valid in Hanafi fiqh and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an unknown acknowledgee (maqirr lahu).

Core Idea
An iqrar of an unknown right is valid if the unknown is the object; but if the recipient is unknown, the iqrar is disputed.

The second condition in the matn states that the iqrar may be for a known or unknown right. This lesson explores the distinction: ignorance of the amount or nature of the debt (maqirr bihi) does not invalidate the iqrar, because a person may legitimately owe an unspecified amount. However, ignorance of the person to whom the right is owed (maqirr lahu) creates a problem, as the claimant cannot be identified. The Hanafi masters differ on the severity of this problem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types
The text distinguishes between the object of acknowledgment (maqirr bihi) and the acknowledgee (maqirr lahu). First, consider the object: known (e.g., specific amount) or unknown (e.g., 'something').
The matn says 'ma'lum aw majhul' referring to the right.
2
Validity of unknown object
The text gives examples: if someone says 'I owe something' or 'a right', the iqrar is valid. This is because the debtor may actually owe an unspecified amount due to tort liability or unresolved accounts.
The commentator lists scenarios: property damage of unknown value, injury with unknown compensation, remaining balance of an account.
3
Problem with unknown acknowledgee
In contrast, if the maqirr lahu is unknown, the iqrar is generally problematic. The text gives two levels: excessive vagueness ('to one of the people') and mild vagueness ('to one of these two').
The unknown cannot be a lawful claimant (mustahiqq).
4
Intra-Hanafi dispute
According to Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi, any unknown maqirr lahu invalidates the iqrar. According to Shaykh al-Islam al-Hilwani and al-Natafi, if the vagueness is not excessive (e.g., limited to a few people), the iqrar is valid, and the muqir should be asked to recall.
Al-Zayla'i reports both views.
5
Practical application
The dispute affects whether the claimant is entitled to demand payment. Under the permissive view, if the muqir later identifies the recipient, the iqrar becomes binding.
The permissive view is based on the possibility that the muqir has simply forgotten.

Source Text

This passage introduces the book on iqrar (acknowledgment) by defining its essential conditions. The muqir (acknowledger) must be mukallaf (sane, adult). However, a discerning child or idiot who is permitted to trade can validly acknowledge matters necessary for trade. The object of acknowledgment (maqirr bihi) can be known or unknown on the part of the muqir; for example, if he owes a specific amount or an unspecified debt. This is because a person may legitimately owe an unknown amount. But the acknowledgee (maqirr lahu) must be identifiable; if he is totally unknown (e.g., 'to one of the people'), the iqrar is invalid because the claimant cannot be determined. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi holds that any vagueness regarding the acknowledgee invalidates the iqrar. Shaykh al-Islam al-Hilwani and al-Natafi distinguish: if the vagueness is excessive (e.g., 'to one of all people'), it is invalid; if mild (e.g., 'to one of these two'), it is valid, and the muqir should be asked to recall.

Key Terms
حق معلوم (haqq ma'lum): A right that is known and specified in amount or description
حق مجهول (haqq majhul): A right that is unknown in amount or description on the part of the muqir
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person to whom the acknowledgment is made (the acknowledgee/claimant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the difference between an unknown right (maqirr bihi) and an unknown acknowledgee (maqirr lahu)?
A) Both invalidate the iqrar.
B) Unknown object is valid; unknown recipient is problematic and disputed.
C) Both are valid if the muqir is honest.
D) Unknown object is invalid; unknown recipient is valid.

Key Takeaway
An iqrar of an unknown right is valid if the unknown is the object; if the recipient is unknown, Hanafi scholars differ on validity depending on the degree of vagueness.

Ruling on Clarifying Unknown Confession and Oath
Ruling on clarification of unknown confession and oath credibility with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on clarifying an unknown item in a confession and explain the confessor's credibility with oath, and distinguish the Hanafi view from that of the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools on the interpretation of 'right'.

Core Idea
A confessor must clarify unknown items of value and is believed with oath; immediate interpretation of 'right' as 'right of Islam' is accepted based on custom.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on confessions involving unknown items. The source text establishes that an unknown confession is valid because it is information (ikhbar), not a disposition, but the confessor must specify anything of value. The confessor is given credibility with an oath if the claimant demands more. The lesson also examines a dispute with three other imams over whether a confessor can later interpret 'I have a right' as a religious right, a case where the Hanafi school accepts the interpretation only if stated immediately, relying on custom ('urf). Understanding this debate sharpens appreciation for Hanafi procedural reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
Read the opening statement: the confessor (muqr) is obligated to clarify any unknown item of value in his confession. He is also believed with his oath if the claimant claims more than his statement. Note that the validity of an unknown confession rests on it being ikhbar (information), not insha' (creation of ownership).
This foundational rule distinguishes Hanafi from schools that may view unknown confessions as invalid.
2
Understand the exceptions and comparisons
The text compares the case to freeing one of two slaves then forgetting – the confessor is not compelled to clarify if it would invalidate the act. But if the object (muqr bih) is unknown, compulsion does not lead to invalidation. Also, if the confessee (muqr lah) is unknown (e.g., 'I owe one of us'), the confession is invalid because the defendant is unknown.
These comparisons help map when clarification is required or not.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Focus on the case: someone says 'I have a right' and then interprets it as 'right of Islam'. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad do not accept this interpretation in any form because 'right' implies a legal obligation. Hanafis accept it only if the interpretation is immediate (connectedly), based on custom ('urf). Discuss the role of custom in Hanafi usul.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): Hanafis enable the confessor to escape liability by immediate clarification; the other schools do not.
4
Apply the ruling to similar scenarios
Consider a modern scenario: someone confesses 'I owe you $500' but later says 'I meant a religious promise, not a legal debt'. Under Hanafi law, if he says this immediately, he is believed; if later, he is not. Also, if the item is trivial (e.g., a grain), clarification is not required.
This step reinforces the practical edge of the ruling.

( وصدق" المقر مع حلفِهِ إن ادعى المقر له أكثر منه الحق، ولا يجبر على البيان لأنه قد يؤدي إلى إبطاله، فصار نظير ما إذا أعتق أحد عبديه ثم نسيه. بخلاف جهالة المقربه ؛ لأن الإجبار على البيان لا يؤدي إلى إبطال حقه، وبخلاف إعتاق أحد العبدين ؛ لأنَّ العتق لم ينزل في المحل، فلا يؤدي الإجبار إلى إبطال حقه ؛ ولأنَّ المُقَرَّ لهما إذ اتفقا على الأخذ من المقرّ واصطلحا بينهما أمكن دعواهما، قال في «الكافي»: وهو الأصح. انتهى. ولو كان المقر له مجهولاً بأن قال : لك على أحدنا ألف درهم لا يصح؛ لأنَّ المقضي عليه مجهول. كذا في «النهاية». [١) قوله : ولزمه؛ أي لزم المقرّ فيما أقر بمجهول بيان ما جهل، أي بيان المجهول، حتى لو امتنع عن البيان أجبره القاضي بما له قيمة؛ لأنه أخبر عن الواجب في ذمته، وما لا قيمة له لا يجب فيها كحبة من الحنطة، فلا يقبل قوله، بل يحمل على الرجوع عن إقراره. [٢] قوله : وصدق...الخ؛ يعني أنَّ المقرَّ صدق مع يمينه إن ادعى المقر له أكثر مما بينه المقر بلا برهان؛ لإنكاره الزيادة، والقول قول المنكر. وقال القهستاني في «شرح النقاية»: والكلام مشير إلى أنه لو أنكر الإقرار بمجهول، وأريد إقامة البينة عليه لم تقبل؛ لأنَّ جهالة المشهود به تمنع صحة الشهادة، وتمامه في «الجواهر» و«التحفة». انتهى.
And it is incumbent upon him to clarify what is unknown regarding that which has value, and the confessor is believed with his oath if the claimant claims more than it. And it is incumbent upon him to clarify what is unknown regarding that which has value; the validity of confession of the unknown is based on it being information (ikhbar) not a creation of ownership (insha' tamlik). (And the confessor is believed with his oath if the claimant claims more than it, and he is not compelled to clarify because it may lead to its invalidation, so it becomes similar to the case where he freed one of his two slaves then forgot. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said: He is not believed in both cases, because his word indicates obligation in the dhimma, and there is no obligation here. End. [2] His saying: 'And he is believed...etc.'; meaning that the confessor is believed with his oath if the claimant claims more than what the confessor stated without proof, because of his denial of the increase, and the statement is the statement of the denier. Al-Qahistani said in Sharh al-Niqaya: And the speech indicates that if he denies the confession of the unknown and wants to establish evidence against him, it is not accepted, because the ignorance of the thing witnessed prevents the validity of testimony. And its completion is in al-Jawahir and al-Tuhfa. End.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute discussing the Hanafi ruling on the obligation to clarify an unknown confession and the credibility of the confessor with an oath. The text begins by stating the ruling: it is incumbent upon the confessor (muqr) to specify any unknown item of value in his confession, and he is believed with his oath if the claimant claims more than what he stated. This is because the confession of the unknown is valid as it is an information (ikhbar), not a creation of ownership. The commentary then compares this to a case where one frees one of his two slaves but forgets which—he cannot be compelled to clarify because it might invalidate the freeing. However, for the object of confession (muqr bih), ignorance does not hinder compulsion because it does not lead to invalidation. Also, if the confessee is unknown (e.g., 'I owe one of us'), the confession is invalid because the defendant is unknown. The commentary further explains that if the confessor refuses to clarify, the qadi compels him only for items with value; trivial items like a grain of wheat are not considered. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali views are presented: they do not believe the confessor in the case of 'right' interpreted as 'right of Islam' unless stated immediately, whereas the Hanafi position accepts it if said connectedly due to custom. The Hanafi school holds that the confessor is believed with oath because the claim is a denial of increase, and the burden of proof is on the claimant. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on the confessor's statement and the importance of clarifying unknown debts.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or debt; it is an information (ikhbar) not a create of ownership in Hanafi law.
مجهول (majhool): Something unknown in the confession, such as an unspecified amount or item; its clarification is required if it has value.
مقر (muqr): The person making the confession; he is believed with oath when the claimant claims more than his statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He is believed only if he provides witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an unknown confession is valid but the confessor must clarify items of value; he is believed with oath, and immediate interpretation of ambiguous terms like 'right' is accepted based on custom.

Minimal Quantities for Vague Property Confessions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on quantifying vague confessions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi views on the minimum for 'great wealth' in a confession and apply Sarakhsi's preferred individual-based view to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The minimum for 'great wealth' in confessions is not fixed; it depends on the confessor's own wealth status per the most correct Hanafi view.

When someone confesses a debt using a vague term like 'property' or 'great wealth', how much must they pay? The Hanafi school has a nuanced approach: a dirham is the minimum for 'property', but for 'great wealth', the jurists disagree on whether a fixed nisab applies or the confessor's personal circumstances matter. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the intra-madhhab dispute and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule for 'mal'
The passage first states that if someone confesses 'I owe property' (mal), he is not believed if he claims less than a dirham. Understand why: custom does not consider fractions of a dirham as 'mal'.
This part is agreed upon among Hanafis.
2
State the dispute over 'mal 'azhim'
For 'great wealth', there are three positions: (1) Sahibayn say it must be at least the zakat nisab (e.g., 200 dirhams for silver). (2) A narration from Abu Hanifa says 10 dirhams (theft nisab). (3) The most correct view of Abu Hanifa, according to Sarakhsi, is that it depends on the confessor's own wealth.
The passage also gives specific nisab amounts for gold, silver, and camels.
3
Analyze the proofs for each view
Sahibayn argue that nisab is 'great' in shari'ah and custom. The theft nisab view argues that 10 dirhams is 'great' because it entails severe penalties. Sarakhsi's view is based on the relativity of poverty and wealth.
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: fixed vs. flexible minimum.
4
Apply Sarakhsi's preferred view
Practice: Given a confessor's status (poor or rich), determine whether a given amount is considered 'great'. For a poor man, even 5 dirhams may be great; for a rich man, 200 dirhams might not be.
This view is the mufta bihi.
5
Review the specific numbers for precious metals and camels
The passage states: for gold, minimum 20 dinars; for silver, 200 dirhams; for camels, 25. These are the zakat nisab amounts and are agreed upon when the confession specifies the type.
Note that for camels, qiyas would suggest 5 (zakat on 5 camels is a sheep), but custom overrides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُصَدِّقُ في أقل من درهم في علي مال، ومن النصاب في: علي مال عظيم من الذهب، أو من الفضة، ومن خمس وعشرين في الإبل. [١] قوله: ولا يصدق...الخ؛ يعني إذا قال له: علي مال لم يصدق في أقل من درهم؛ لأنَّ ما دونه من الكسور لا يطلق عليه اسم المال عادة، وهو المعتبر. قال في «البحر»: ولو قال: لفلان علي دار أو عبد لا يلزمه بشيء، أو مال قليل، أو درهم عظيم، أو درهم لزمه درهم. انتهى. فظاهره أنه يلزمه درهم ولا يجبر على بيان. [٢] قوله: ومن النصاب...الخ؛ يعني إذا قال له: علي مال عظيم، وأجبره القاضي على البيان، فقال: عنيت أقل من النصاب، لم يصدق؛ لأنه أقر بمال موصوف بالعظيم، فيعتبر هذا الوصف، والنصاب عظيمٌ في الشرع، حتى يصير صاحبه غنيّاً، وأوجب عليه مواساة الفقراء. وكذا عرفاً، حتى يعد من الأغنياء عادة، وهذا قولهما، وهو مروي عن الإمام، وعنه له أيضاً: إنّه لا يصدق في أقل من عشرة دراهم، وهي نصاب السرقة؛ لأنه عظيم حيث يقطع به اليد المحترمة، ويستباح به البضع المحترم. قال في «النتائج»: قال شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِيُّ له: والأصح على قول أبي حنيفة أنه يبنى على حال المقرّ في الفقر والغناء، فإن القليل عند الفقير عظيم، وأضعاف ذلك عند الغني حقير. انتهى والله أعلم. [٣] قوله: من الذهب...الخ؛ فلا يصدق في أقل من عشرين ديناراً، إذا قال: من الدنانير، وفي أقل من مئتي درهم إذا قال: من الدراهم، وفي أقل من خمس وعشرين إذا قال: من الإبل، فإنَّ خمساً وعشرين أدنى نصاب منها يجب فيه من جنسه. قال البرْجَنْدِي: وكان القياس أن يقدر بخمس، فإنّه يجب فيها شاء، إلا أنه عظيم ( نتائج الأفكار ٧: ٣٠٦).
And he is not believed in less than a dirham regarding 'I owe property', and from the nisab regarding: 'I owe great wealth of gold, or of silver', and from twenty-five regarding camels. [1] His saying: 'And he is not believed...etc.'; meaning if he says: 'I owe him property', he is not believed in less than a dirham, because what is below it of fractions is not called 'property' customarily, and it is the criterion. Al-Bahr said: If he says: 'I owe so-and-so a house or a slave', nothing is binding on him; or 'a little property', or 'a great dirham', or 'a dirham', a dirham is binding. End. So its apparent meaning is that a dirham is binding and he is not compelled to clarify. [2] His saying: 'And from the nisab...etc.'; meaning if he says: 'I owe great wealth', and the qadi compels him to clarify, and he says: 'I meant less than the nisab', he is not believed, because he confessed to property described as great, and this description is considered, and the nisab is great in the law, so that its owner becomes rich, and it obligated him to support the poor. And likewise customarily, so that he is counted among the rich usually. This is their saying (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is narrated from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), and from him also: He is not believed in less than ten dirhams, which is the nisab of theft, because it is great as it entails cutting of the respected hand and making lawful the respected private part. Al-Nata'ij said: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The most correct according to Abu Hanifa is that it is based on the state of the confessor in poverty and wealth, for little is great to the poor, and multiples of that are insignificant to the rich. End, and Allah knows best. [3] His saying: 'Of gold...etc.'; so he is not believed in less than twenty dinars if he says 'of dinars', and in less than two hundred dirhams if he says 'of dirhams', and in less than twenty-five if he says 'of camels', because twenty-five is the minimum nisab of camels which requires a sheep. Al-Barjandi said: The analogy would be to estimate it as five, because it requires a sheep, but it is great (Nata'ij al-Afkar 7:306).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute discussing the minimal amounts for vague property confessions. The text states that if someone confesses 'I owe property' (مال), he is not believed if he claims it is less than a dirham, because customarily fractions of a dirham are not considered property. If he says 'great wealth' (مال عظيم), he is not believed if he interprets it as less than the nisab (e.g., for gold, silver, or camels), because 'great' is defined by the nisab in shari'ah and custom. The text notes a difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the companions hold the nisab as the minimum, while a narration from Abu Hanifa says the minimum is ten dirhams (nisab for theft). However, Sarakhsi reports that the most correct view of Abu Hanifa is to consider the confessor's personal wealth—what is 'great' depends on the individual's status. This intra-madhhab ikhtilaf shows the nuanced approach to interpreting vague terms in confessions. The passage also specifies that for gold, the minimum is twenty dinars; for silver, two hundred dirhams; for camels, twenty-five. These are the nisab amounts for zakat. The text mentions that qiyas would suggest five for camels (as zakat on five camels is a sheep), but custom overrides. This lesson illustrates how Hanafi jurists balance linguistic custom, shari'ah nisab, and individual circumstances in quantifying vague confessions.

Key Terms
مال عظيم (mal 'azhim): Great wealth; in confession, it is quantified by the nisab for zakat or by the confessor's status per Abu Hanifa.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of property that triggers zakat or other obligations; here used as a benchmark for 'great' in confessions.
درهم (dirham): A silver coin; the minimum unit of property in confession below which it is not considered 'mal'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is believed because he knows his own intention.
B) He is not believed because 'great' must be at least the zakat nisab.
C) He is believed if he is poor, but not if he is rich.
D) He is not believed because 'great' in his case may require more than the nisab.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for vague 'great wealth' confessions is that the minimum depends on the individual confessor's financial status, not a fixed shari'ah nisab.

Confession of Great Wealth: Minimum Nisab Value in Non-Zakat Property
Commentary on iqrar of 'great wealth' in non-zakat items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum amount required for a valid confession of 'great wealth' in non-zakat items and explain the Hanafi reasoning (talil) behind it.

Core Idea
A confession of 'great wealth' in non-zakat items is valid only if the amount reaches the value of nisab (200 dirhams).

When someone confesses a debt of 'great wealth' from items like wheat or animals, the court must determine a minimum value for the confession to be binding. The Hanafi school uses the zakat threshold as an objective measure of greatness, but there is an early transmission from Abu Hanifa suggesting a much lower minimum. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is iqrar (confession) of 'great wealth' in non-zakat property. The ruling is that the minimum amount required for validity is the value of nisab (200 dirhams), and the confessor is not believed if he claims less.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the literal meaning of 'great' (عظيم) in common usage, which denotes a significant amount. The nisab for zakat is adopted as an objective benchmark.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'great' implies substantial value; since zakat is the Sharia standard for substantial wealth, the nisab is used here to ensure consistency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a riwayah from Abu Hanifa that the minimum is 10 dirhams, but the relied-upon view is the nisab standard.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Later Hanafi masters (e.g., in al-Kifayah) affirm the nisab standard as the mufta bihi, giving precedence to wider consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قَدْر النصاب قيمة في غير مال الزكاة، ومن ثلاثة نصب في أموال عظام، ودراهم ثلاثة (٣) من أنه يجب فيه الزكاة، وليس بعظيم من حيث أنه لا يجب فيه الزكاة من جنسه، فاعتبر ما ذكر ؛ ليكون عظيماً مطلقاً. انتهى. And from the amount of nisab value in non-zakat property, and from three nisabs in large properties, and three dirhams (3). Because zakat is obligatory on it, and it is not great in the sense that zakat is not obligatory on its type, so he considered what was mentioned to be absolutely great. End. [1] His saying: 'And from the amount of nisab value...etc.'; that is, he is not believed for less than the amount of nisab value in non-zakat property, like wheat and others. So if someone says to him: I have a great wealth of donkey or mule or wheat or others, and the judge forces him to clarify, and he says: I meant less than the amount of nisab value, he is not believed because its value is also great. And it is reported from Imam [Abu Hanifa] that it is estimated at ten dirhams, as in al-Ikhtiyar. And he said in al-Kifayah (2): If he said: I have valuable property, or noble, or serious, or magnificent, al-Natifi said: I have not found it textually stated, and al-Jurjani said: He is liable for two hundred. And if he said: I have a little dirham or a little dinar, then he owes a full dirham or a full dinar because the small may be mentioned for the smallness of its size by the diminutive so it does not decrease from the weight. 6 And if he said: a lot of wheat, then it is five wasaq, and a wasaq is sixty sa', and this is according to their statement because the nisab in the matter of tithe is estimated by this. But according to Abu Hanifa's statement, there is no nisab for wheat, so it refers to the clarification of the confessor. End.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling concerning a confession (iqrar) of a 'great wealth' (مال عظيم) in items that are not subject to zakat, such as wheat, donkeys, etc. The base text states that the minimum for such a confession is the value of nisab (the threshold for zakat), which is approximately 200 dirhams. The commentator explains that if a person confesses to having a 'great wealth' from such items, he is not believed if he later claims he meant less than the nisab value, because the term 'great' itself implies a substantial amount. There is also a report from Imam Abu Hanifa that the minimum is 10 dirhams, but the relied-upon view (as indicated by the text) is the nisab. Additional rulings from al-Kifayah cover similar terms like 'valuable' (نفيس) and 'noble' (كريم), where some jurists like al-Jurjani hold that two hundred dirhams are binding. The passage also addresses the use of diminutives like 'دريهم' (a small dirham) which still require a full dirham by weight. Regarding 'a lot of wheat', the nisab for wheat is 5 wasaq (i.e., 300 sa'), but according to Abu Hanifa, wheat has no nisab, so the confessor's clarification is accepted.

Key Terms
مال عظيم (mal 'azim): A term used in iqrar to denote a significant amount of property; interpreted as at least the value of nisab (200 dirhams) in non-zakat items.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers zakat obligation; for dirhams it is 200 dirhams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Minimum Dirhams in Confession of 'Darahim'
Ruling on minimum liability in iqrar with plural 'darahim'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum number of dirhams owed when someone confesses to owing 'darahim' and explain the linguistic basis for this ruling.

Core Idea
The Arabic plural 'darahim' imposes a minimum of three dirhams.

This short passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that when a person confesses to owing a debt using the plural 'dirhams' without specifying a number, the minimum obligation is three dirhams. This ruling is rooted in the Arabic linguistic principle that the sound feminine plural indicates a minimum of three. The commentator cites authoritative Hanafi texts to confirm this established position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the confessional statement
The confessor uses the plural form 'darahim' without specifying a number. This is the key operative term.
The plural form is the trigger for the default minimum.
2
Apply the linguistic rule
In Arabic, the plural form (جمع) grammatically implies at least three. The sound feminine plural (جمع مؤنث سالم) and broken plurals both share this property.
This is a well-established rule of Arabic grammar accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Determine the ruling
Therefore, the confessor must pay a minimum of three dirhams. He may owe more if he clarifies a higher number, but the default is three.
The obligation is to pay at least three dirhams.
4
Verify authoritative support
The commentator references al-Ikhtiyar, al-Kifayah, and Manh al-Ghaffar, all reliable Hanafi sources, confirming this as the mufta bihi position.
These texts represent the established school doctrine.

The commentator cites three sources: al-Ikhtiyar, al-Kifayah, and Manh al-Ghaffar, confirming that this is the established ruling in the Hanafi school.
