Confession of 'Many Dirhams': The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Minimum
Dispute on interpreting 'many dirhams' in confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the minimum for 'many dirhams' in a confession and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
For 'many dirhams', Abu Hanifa says ten; the Sahibayn say nisab (200) — linguistic form vs. legal context.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the interpretation of ambiguous terms in confessions can lead to important disputes. This lesson explores the famous disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples (Sahibayn) regarding the meaning of 'many dirhams' (دراهم كثيرة) when a person confesses a debt. The dispute hinges on whether to use strict Arabic grammatical rules or the established threshold for zakat (nisab) as a benchmark. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze how different legal presumptions lead to different outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the legal scenario: a person says 'I owe you many dirhams.' What is the minimum amount he must pay?
2
Locate the two positions
State Abu Hanifa's view (10 dirhams) and the Sahibayn's view (200 dirhams) from the text.
The matn states Abu Hanifa's view; the commentary explains the Sahibayn's.
3
Identify each side's dalil
For Abu Hanifa: linguistic (jam' al-kathrah implies ten). For Sahibayn: contextual (nisab as abundance).
Dalil types: linguistic vs. analogical reasoning from zakat thresholds.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under Abu Hanifa, he owes 10 dirhams; under Sahibayn, he owes 200 dirhams.
This difference affects the outcome of the case significantly.
5
Extend to other property types
Apply the same dispute to clothes, cows, sheep, and wheat. According to the Sahibayn, the minimum is the respective nisab for each type.
The text mentions camels, cows, sheep, and wheat.

Book of Iqrar / Its Definition and Rulings

And many dirhams: ten, and likewise such and such dirham: one dirham. 239 And many dirhams: ten, this according to Abu Hanifa because the plural of multitude has a minimum of ten (1). And according to them (the Sahibayn), he is not believed for less than the nisab. (And likewise such and such dirham: one dirham because it is the minimum of the sound plural, so it becomes certain, and the excess is doubtful. He said in al-Hidayah: except if he clarifies more than that, because the wording allows it, and it refers to the customary weight. End.) And if he said: I owe him double dirhams, then six dirhams are due because the minimum of the plural is three, and its double is six. And if he said: dirhams multiplied, the wording and its minimum is three, so it becomes nine, then with multiplication it becomes eighteen. As in al-Tabyin (3) by al-Zayla'i. [1] His saying: 'and many dirhams'; meaning if he says: I owe him many dirhams, ten dirhams are obligatory upon him, and if he says: I meant less than this, he is not believed. [2] His saying: 'this...etc.'; meaning his statement 'I owe him many dirhams' is interpreted as ten dirhams according to the Imam's school, and its reasoning: because ten is the furthest limit that the name 'plural' reaches, so it is the most in terms of wording, so it is applied. [3] His saying: 'And according to them he is not believed for less than the nisab'; which is two hundred; because the owner of the nisab is abundant, until he is obligated to give charity to others, unlike what is below the nisab, for its owner is poor; and therefore he is not obligated to give charity to others. And upon this difference, if he says: I owe him many clothes, or many cows, or many sheep, or much wheat, it refers to the minimum nisab from which zakat is taken of its type according to them, which is twenty-five camels, thirty cows, forty sheep, and five wasaq of wheat.

Commentary
This passage covers the interpretation of 'many dirhams' (دراهم كثيرة) in a confession. There is a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn). Abu Hanifa holds that 'many' (كثيرة) refers to the plural of multitude (جمع الكثرة) which has a minimum of ten. Therefore, the confessor must pay ten dirhams. The Sahibayn, however, argue that the minimum is the nisab (200 dirhams) because the term 'many' implies abundance, and the nisab is the threshold for zakat, indicating wealth. The passage also addresses other cases: if he says 'double dirhams' (دراهم مضاعفة), he owes six (three doubled). For 'multiplied dirhams' (أضعافاً), the minimum is three, then multiplied to make nine, then multiplied again? Actually the text says it becomes eighteen? It might be a complex calculation. The commentary then clarifies that according to Abu Hanifa, ten is the maximum limit of the plural form, so that is what is applied. For the Sahibayn, the minimum is the nisab. This difference extends to other types of property, such as clothes, cows, sheep, and wheat, where the minimum nisab for each type is used according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
جمع الكثرة (jam' al-kathrah): The plural of multitude in Arabic, which has a minimum of ten, according to some grammarians. Used here to determine the minimum for 'many dirhams'.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum threshold of wealth for zakat; for dirhams it is 200 dirhams.

The Default Interpretation of 'Such and Such Dirham'
Ruling with linguistic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default interpretation of the phrase 'such and such dirham' in Hanafi law and explain the linguistic basis for that ruling.

Core Idea
An indefinite singular noun in a confession defaults to one.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a person uses an indefinite singular noun in a confession, the minimum number applies. This lesson explores the ruling for the phrase 'كذا درهماً' (such and such dirham) and its linguistic justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act is a confession (iqrar) using the phrase 'such and such dirham' (كذا درهماً).
2
State the Ruling
The debtor must pay one dirham, because the word 'dirham' is singular indefinite.
3
Locate the Proof
The proof is the linguistic principle that an indefinite singular noun indicates the smallest possible number, which is one.
The text cites al-Hidayah for this rule.
4
Note the Exception
If the confessor clarifies a larger amount, that amount will be binding instead.

The Minimal Number in Ambiguous Iqrar: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Commentary on ambiguous numbers in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi minimal numbers for the word 'kadha' in various constructions in iqrar and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
'Kadha' is a number, not an indefinite; its minimum is two unless compounded.

When someone says 'I owe him kadha dirhams,' what does 'kadha' mean? The Hanafi school treats it as a number with a minimum, while the Shafi'i school sees it as an indefinite singular. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind the Hanafi position and its practical consequences, drawing from the commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous expression
Recognize that 'kadha' is used in iqrar to indicate an unspecified number. The debtor's statement creates a liability that must be interpreted according to linguistic norms and legal principles.
The principle of bara'at al-dhimmah (presumption of innocence) requires taking the minimum possible.
2
Recall the Hanafi linguistic principle
Hanafis hold that 'kadha' is a kinayah (metonym) for a number, and the smallest number in Arabic is two—one is not considered a real number (al-wahid laysa bi 'adad).
This principle is explicit in al-Jami' al-Saghir and other Hanafi sources.
3
Apply to 'kadha' alone and 'kadha kadha'
For 'kadha' alone, the minimum is two dirhams. For 'kadha kadha' without waw, the least two-number compound is eleven. With waw ('kadha wa kadha'), the minimum is twenty-one. These are derived from the pattern of Arabic numbers.
The passage notes an alternative Hanafi view that the minimum non-compound with accusative is twenty (ishrin), and with genitive one hundred.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i view
Shafi'is and Hanbalis treat 'kadha' as an indefinite singular, so the minimum is one dirham. The Hanafi response is that 'kadha' is specifically a number term, and singular is not a number.
The practical difference: Hanafi judge obligates at least two dirhams; Shafi'i judge obligates one.
5
Analyse the repetition patterns
When 'kadha' is repeated three times without waw, the minimum remains eleven (no parallel for three numbers). With waw, triple repetition gives 121, quadruple gives 1,121, and so on, increasing by powers of ten.
This ensures certainty in liability; the default is that the liability is free, so only the minimal certain amount is taken.

Source Text

And 'kadha kadha' means eleven, and 'kadha wa kadha' means twenty-one; because 'kadha kadha' is a representation of two numbers, and the least two numbers mentioned without the conjunction waw is eleven, and the least two numbers mentioned with the waw is twenty-one. Because 'kadha' is ambiguous, and 'dirhaman' interprets it. In al-Dhakhira from al-Jami' al-Saghir: two dirhams, because 'kadha' denotes a number, and the least number is two, because one is not considered a number. In Sharh al-Mukhtar: it is said that he is obligated to pay twenty, which is based on analogy, because 'kadha' is used as a representation of a number, and the least non-composite number after which 'dirham' is in the accusative is twenty. And if he mentions it in the genitive case, it is related from Muhammad that he is obligated to pay one hundred, because it is the least number after which 'dirham' is mentioned in the genitive case. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, he is obligated one dirham in the nominative and accusative cases, and in the genitive case its fraction. Thus in al-Ramz. And if he says, 'upon him is a great dirham', he is obligated one dirham, because the dirham is known in its own value, so its value does not increase by his saying 'great', because it is a description. Thus in al-Tabyin. And al-Barjandi said in Sharh al-Niqayah: In Fatawa Qadikhan, if he says 'to someone upon me is kadha, dirhaman', he is obligated two dirhams, because 'kadha' is used for a number, and the least number is two. What the author mentioned agrees with what is in al-Hidayah, and it is the more evident, because one is also called a number, even if philosophers do not apply the name of number to it. End. So reflect on it and do not be of the heedless. [1] His statement: 'wa kadha kadha... etc.' means: if he says 'upon him is kadha kadha dirhaman' without waw, he is obligated eleven dirhams, because he mentioned two ambiguous numbers without the conjunction, and the least of that from the interpreted number is eleven, and the most is nineteen, so the least is obligated without clarification, and the increase depends on his clarification. According to al-Shafi'i, he is obligated one dirham. Kitab al-Iqrar: its definition and rulings. And if he repeats three times without waw, then eleven, and with waw then one hundred and twenty-one. And if he quadruples, add a thousand. His statement: 'and if he repeats three times without waw, then eleven' because there is no parallel for three without waw, so the nearest to it is two without waw, meaning eleven. And with 'waw' then one hundred and twenty-one. And if he quadruples, add a thousand, meaning if he quadruples the word 'kadha' with the waw, then it is one thousand one hundred and twenty-one. And if he says 'upon him is kadha wa kadha' with the conjunction, he is obligated twenty-one dirhams, because he separated between them with the conjunction, and the least of that from the interpreted number is twenty-one, and the most is ninety-nine, so the least is obligated without clarification, and the increase depends on his clarification. According to al-Shafi'i, he is obligated two dirhams. Thus in al-Ramz. [2] His statement: 'and if he repeats three times... etc.' means: if he repeats the word 'kadha' three times without mentioning the conjunction, by saying without waw: 'upon him is kadha kadha kadha dirhaman', then he is obligated eleven also, because there is no parallel for it among the numerical expressions, so one of them is taken as repetition due to the necessity of not having three numbers combined without a conjunction, and the intended is eleven because it is the nearest. [3] His statement: 'if he quadruples... etc.' means: if he quadruples the word 'kadha' with the tripling of the waw, by saying 'upon him is kadha wa kadha wa kadha wa kadha dirhaman', he is obligated one thousand one hundred and twenty-one, because it is the least that is expressed by four numbers with the waw, so it is taken as the least certain rather than the most, because the principle is that the liability is free. And if he repeats five times, add ten thousand; if six times, add one hundred thousand; if seven times, add one thousand thousand; and whenever the number conjoined with waw increases, what is customary is added, ad infinitum. Thus in al-Bahr and others. And if he says 'kadha kadha dirhaman wa dinaran', then he is obligated eleven of each equally, because he mentioned an ambiguous number and two types shared in it, so he is obligated half of each, unlike if he says 'kadha'.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the ruling about ambiguous numerical expressions in iqrar (legal declaration). It explains the Hanafi position on interpreting the phrase 'kadha kadha' and its variants. The author begins by stating that when someone says 'kadha kadha dirhaman' without conjunction, the minimal obligation is eleven dirhams, based on the principle that the least number of two ambiguous units without 'waw' is eleven. If said with 'waw' ('kadha wa kadha'), it becomes twenty-one. This is because 'kadha' is a kinayah (metonym) for an unspecified number, and the least 'adad' (number) in Arabic usage is two (according to some), but here the context of two numbers together without conjunction forces the interpretation of a compound number like 11. The text then discusses differences within the Hanafi school: according to al-Jami' al-Saghir, the least number is 2, so 'kadha' alone means two dirhams; but according to Sharh al-Mukhtar, the minimal non-compound number after which 'dirham' is accusative (mansub) is 20 (like 'ishrin), and if genitive (makhfud), it could be 100 (as per Muhammad). Then the passage contrasts the Shafi'i and Hanbali view (Imam al-Shafi'i and Ahmad) that 'kadha' without specification means one dirham, while Hanafis require two or more. This is a classic inter-madhhab dispute: Shafi'is take 'kadha' as indefinite singular, while Hanafis see it as a number with minimal two. The commentary then explains the repetition of 'kadha' in triple and quadruple forms, with or without 'waw'. If said three times without 'waw' (e.g., 'kadha kadha kadha'), it still yields eleven because three consecutive ambiguous numbers without conjunction have no parallel in normal usage; the closest is the two-number compound 'eleven'. With 'waw' (e.g., 'kadha wa kadha wa kadha'), the minimal is 121, because three numbers conjoined with 'waw' give the pattern of 100 + 20 + 1 = 121. For quadruple with 'waw', the minimal is 1,121, and so on, increasing exponentially (adding thousands, lakhs, etc.) ad infinitum. This is a technical ruling to ensure certainty in debts; the default is that the liability is free (bara'ah), so only the minimal certain amount is obliged. The passage also mentions that if the creditor says 'kadha kadha dirhaman wa dinaran' (two types), the debtor owes eleven of each equally, because the ambiguous number is shared between two types, so it is divided equally. This shows the Hanafi method of applying qiyas and istihsan to determine the least probable number when the debtor is ambiguous, protecting the debtor from excessive claims while giving the creditor the minimum. The principle is 'al-asl bara'at al-dhimmah' (presumption of innocence of liability).

Key Terms
كذا (kadha): An ambiguous word used as a kinayah (metonym) for an unspecified number in financial declarations.
إقرار (iqrar): A legal admission of a right owed by one person to another, often used in debts.
عدد (adad): Number; in this passage, Arabic numbers are classified as simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab), with minimal values determined by usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'kadha' as a number with a minimum of two, and its doubled forms yield specific minimal obligations based on Arabic numerical patterns.

Ruling on Ambiguous Numerical Expressions in Iqrar
Sharh expanding rulings on 'kadha kadha' expressions in iqrar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ambiguous 'kadha' expressions in iqrar and apply the principle of minimum interpretation to determine the owed amount.

Core Idea
Ambiguous numbers in iqrar are interpreted as the smallest plausible amount due to the principle of immunity (al-bara'a al-asliyya).

This passage, from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, expands on the matn's rulings for acknowledging debts using the ambiguous term 'kadha'. The author clarifies how the presence or absence of the conjunctive 'waw' changes the owed amount, always defaulting to the minimum to protect the debtor. You'll also encounter a cross-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school on the interpretation of 'kadha kadha' with 'waw'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an iqrar (acknowledgment) using the ambiguous term 'kadha'. The ruling is that a specific amount becomes wajib (obligatory) upon the debtor, determined by the pattern of expression.
Focus on the three main scenarios: without waw, with waw (two terms), and with waw (three or more terms).
2
Locate the underlying principle
The governing principle is 'al-asl fi al-dhimam al-bara'a' (the default is freedom from liability). Therefore, the minimum possible amount is taken to avoid imposing extra without proof.
This principle is a key Hanafi usul maxim applied in iqrar and other areas.
3
Analyze the linguistic reasoning for each scenario
Without waw: 'kadha kadha' is interpreted as 11 (the smallest two-digit number formed by repetition). With waw between two terms: 'kadha wa kadha' is interpreted as 21 (the smallest number formed by two consecutive tens conjoined). For three or more terms with waw: the pattern continues (e.g., 121, 1121) by multiplying by 10, 100, etc., as customary.
The repetition without waw is analogous to 'eleven' in Arabic, which is literally 'ten and one' but said as one word.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with the Shafi'i school
The Shafi'i school holds that 'kadha kadha' with waw implies only two dirhams (as per al-Ramz). The Hanafi school rejects this, arguing that the minimum composite number is 21.
The practical difference: a Hanafi judge would impose 21 dirhams, while a Shafi'i judge would impose 2.
5
Apply the ruling to mixed scenarios
If the ambiguous number is attached to two kinds (e.g., dirham and dinar), half of each is owed. If each kind is specified separately, the full number is owed for each.
This follows the same principle of minimum certainty: the ambiguous number is divided equally.

As in 'al-Bahr' and others. Unlike if he said: 'kadha dirham, and kadha kadha dinar,' where he owes one of each number for each kind. And similarly for precise numbers: if he says: 'I owe eleven dirhams and a dinar,' half of each is required. And if he said: 'I owe eleven dirhams and eleven dinars,' he owes eleven of each. The principle is: whenever he mentions an amount and attaches it to two kinds of property, half of each is required, just as if he made an acknowledgment to two men dividing equally. Example: if he said: 'I owe one hundred mithqals of gold and silver, or a kurr of wheat and barley,' half of each is required. As in 'al-Tabyin' and others.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn's rulings regarding ambiguous numerical expressions in iqrar (acknowledgment of debt). The author explains three scenarios: (1) When the acknowledging person says 'kadha kadha kadha' without the conjunctive 'wa' (waw), the minimum number understood is eleven (as two repetitions of 'kadha' yield eleven, the nearest composite). (2) When he says 'kadha wa kadha wa kadha' with waw, the least number expressed by three such terms is 121. (3) If he says four such terms with waw, the least is 1121. The principle is 'al-asl fi al-dhimam al-bara'a' (the default is freedom from obligation), so the minimum possible amount is taken. The passage then extends this to any number of terms, each addition multiplying by ten thousand, hundred thousand, etc., as customary. A further ruling covers mixing dirhams and dinars: if the number is ambiguous and applied to both, half of each is owed; if each kind is specified separately, the full number is owed for each. Finally, a general principle is stated: when an amount is attributed to two categories of property, half of each must be given, analogous to an acknowledgment to two people.

Key Terms
كذا (kadha): An ambiguous term referring to an unspecified number in expressions of acknowledgment.
عدد مفسر (adad mufassar): A precise number explicitly stated (e.g., eleven).
بلا واو (bila waw): Without the conjunctive 'waw', meaning the terms are said consecutively without conjunction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 11 dirhams

C) 21 dirhams
D) 111 dirhams

Key Takeaway
For ambiguous 'kadha' expressions in iqrar, the Hanafi school defaults to the minimum plausible number (without waw: 11, with waw: 21, etc.) based on the principle of al-bara'a al-asliyya.

علي' and 'قبلي' as Acknowledgment of Debt (Majority View)
Commentary on the matn's ruling that 'علي' and 'قبلي' are acknowledgments of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that 'علي' and 'قبلي' are acknowledgments of debt, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding 'قبلي', and apply the linguistic reasoning that supports the majority view.

Core Idea
'علي' and 'قبلي' are acknowledgments of debt; 'قبلي' is derived from guarantee and predominantly used for debt.

In Hanafi fiqh, the words used in an acknowledgment (iqrar) determine the nature of the liability. This lesson examines two common terms: 'علي' (upon me) and 'قبلي' (with me). While both are generally considered acknowledgments of debt, there is a minority view within the Hanafi school that 'قبلي' could indicate a trust. Understanding the linguistic basis and the preferred ruling is essential for properly evaluating such statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the words
Recognize that 'علي' and 'قبلي' are the words under discussion. 'علي' clearly means obligation, while 'قبلي' has a dual potential.
2
Locate the ruling and proof
The matn rules that both are acknowledgments of debt. The proof is linguistic: 'علي' indicates obligation, and 'قبلي' derives from 'qabala' meaning guarantee, linking it to debt.
The author cites the chapter of kafala for the derivation.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some copies of Mukhtasar al-Quduri hold that 'قبلي' acknowledges trust (amanah), because the word can mean both debt and trust.
The minority argues that trust is the lesser claim and thus more certain to attribute to the speaker.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The majority view, cited from al-Mabsut, is that 'قبلي' indicates debt because its usage in debts is far more frequent and predominant.
The author declares this view 'al-asah' (most correct).
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, when a person says 'علي كذا' or 'قبلي كذا', it is a binding acknowledgment of debt unless he immediately and connectly clarifies it is a trust.
The condition of connection is covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وعلي وقبلي إقرار بدين ; يعني لو قال : له عليّ أو قبلي كذا، فقد أقر بالدين ; لأنَّ كلمته : علي للوجوب، وقبلي ينبئ عن الضمان، يقال: قبل فلان عن فلان: أي ضَمِن، والكفيل يسمى قبيلاً، والصك يسمى قبالة. كما ذكرناه في «كتاب الكفالة». وفي بعض نسخ «مختصر القُدُروي» في قوله : قبلي ؛ إنه إقرار بالأمانة ; لأنَّ اللفظ يتناول الأمانة والدين كليهما ، يقال : ليس لي قبل فلان دين وليس قبل فلان وديعة. وكذا إذا قال : لا حق لي قبل فلان ; يراد به الإبراء عن الدين والأمانة جميعاً، والأمانة أقلهما لعدم الضمان فيه فيحمل قولُ المُقِرِّ عليه ; لكون الأدنى هو المتيقن، والأول هو المذكور في «المبسوط»، وهو الأصح ; لأنَّ استعماله في الديون أغلب وأكثر فكان الحمل عليه أوجه. وقوله : وصدق إن وَصَلَ به، هو وديعة، وإن فصل لا : لأنَّ ظاهره الإقرار بالدين، فقوله : هو وديعة ; يكون بيان تغيير بتأويل أن عليه حفظ الوديعة ، وهو يصح موصولاً لا مفصولاً كالاستثناء والتخصيص.
And his statement: 'And 'علي' and 'قبلي' are an acknowledgment of debt' means: if he said: 'He has upon me (علي) or with me (قبلي) such-and-such,' then he has acknowledged debt; because his word 'علي' indicates obligation, and 'قبلي' indicates guarantee. It is said: 'qabila fulan 'an fulan' means he became a guarantor, and the guarantor is called 'qabil', and the document is called 'qabala'. As we mentioned in the chapter of kafala. In some copies of 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri' regarding his saying 'قبلي', it is an acknowledgment of trust; because the word encompasses both trust and debt. It is said: 'I have no debt with so-and-so' and 'I have no trust with so-and-so'. Similarly, if he said: 'I have no claim (haqq) with so-and-so,' it is understood as a release from both debt and trust, and the trust is the lesser of the two because there is no liability in it, so the statement of the acknowledging person is accepted for him because the lesser is certain. The first view is the one mentioned in 'al-Mabsut', and it is the most correct; because its usage in debts is more frequent and predominant, so construing it as debt is more appropriate. And his statement: 'And he is believed if he connects it, that it is a trust, and if he separates it, he is not' because the apparent meaning is acknowledgment of debt, so his saying 'it is a trust' becomes a clarifying statement that changes [the meaning] with the interpretation that he is obligated to preserve the trust, and this is valid when connected, not when separated, like exception and specification.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn's statement that 'علي' and 'قبلي' are acknowledgments of debt. The author first explains the linguistic basis: 'علي' denotes obligation, and 'قبلي' (from qabala) implies guarantee, so both are used for debt. He acknowledges a minority view from some copies of Mukhtasar al-Quduri that 'قبلي' indicates trust (amanah), because the word can be used for both debt and trust. However, the author dismisses this by noting that in 'al-Mabsut' the correct view is that it indicates debt, since usage in debt is more common. The second part discusses the situation where the acknowledging person later says 'it is a trust' (هو وديعة). This is accepted only if said in a connected manner (wasl), i.e., without a gap, like an exception. If disconnected (fasl), it is not accepted because the apparent meaning is debt and a subsequent contradictory statement is not given credence.

Key Terms
علي (ala): A word indicating obligation or debt in the context of iqrar.
قبلي (qabli): A word meaning 'with me' or 'owed by me', primarily indicating debt according to the majority, but also used for trust.
وصله / فصله (wasala / fasal): Connected vs. disconnected speech; in iqrar, a subsequent explanation is only valid if immediately connected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view (al-asah) in the Hanafi school, if a person says 'قبلي ألف درهم', what does this statement establish?
A) A trust (amanah) of 1000 dirhams
B) A debt (dayn) of 1000 dirhams
C) An ambiguous statement requiring clarification
D) A gift (hibah) of 1000 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that both 'علي' and 'قبلي' are acknowledgments of debt, with the majority view that 'قبلي' indicates debt due to predominant usage.

The Condition of Connection: Trust Claim After Acknowledgment
Commentary on the condition of connection for trust claim after acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a subsequent explanation (e.g., 'it is a trust') is accepted only if it is immediately connected (wasl) to the acknowledgment, and distinguish between connected and disconnected speech.

Core Idea
A subsequent trust claim after 'علي' or 'قبلي' is accepted only if connected; otherwise, the original acknowledgment stands.

The passage then turns to a crucial procedural rule: if a person acknowledges a debt using 'علي' or 'قبلي', and then immediately adds that it is actually a trust (amanah), this explanation is accepted as a valid interpretation. However, if there is a separation (fasl) between the acknowledgment and the explanation, the explanation is disregarded. This parallels the rules for exception (istithna) and specification (takhsis) in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the situation: a person says 'علي كذا' or 'قبلي كذا' and then says 'هو وديعة' (it is a trust).
2
Distinguish between connected and disconnected
Connected (wasl) means the explanation is uttered immediately without any break. Disconnected (fasl) means there is a separation, even a brief pause or a new sentence.
The distinction is critical for the ruling.
3
State the ruling
If connected, the person is believed and it is considered a trust. If disconnected, the explanation is not accepted and the debt acknowledgment stands.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The apparent meaning of the original acknowledgment is debt. A subsequent statement that changes the meaning is akin to an exception or specification, which must be immediate to be part of the same legal act.
The author draws an analogy to istithna and takhsis.
5
Apply the analogy
Just as in contracts, an exception or condition must be stated without separation to be effective, so too in acknowledgments, a contradictory explanation must be connected.

Interpretation of 'Ali' and 'Qabli' in Acknowledgment
Commentary on the matn about 'ali' and 'qabli' as acknowledgment of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling for 'ali' and 'qabli' in acknowledgments and apply the condition of connectivity for a change to a trust explanation.

Core Idea
'Ali' and 'qabli' default to debt; a connected explanation can change them to trust.

In Hanafi fiqh, the words used in acknowledgments are interpreted according to their predominant linguistic usage. This lesson examines the default meaning of 'ali' and 'qabli' when a person says 'He has a claim upon me' or 'I owe him', and explores when such an acknowledgment can be reinterpreted as a deposit or trust. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the rules of connectivity and the intra-Hanafi disagreement about the word 'qabli'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling for 'ali' and 'qabli'
State that both words prima facie indicate a debt (dayn). 'Ali' indicates obligation (wujub), and 'qabli' indicates guarantee (dhaman). This is the position of the majority and is recorded in al-Mabsut.
The default is important because it establishes liability.
2
Examine the condition for changing to trust
If the confessor immediately and without pause says 'it is a deposit' (wadi'ah) after 'ali' or 'qabli', he is believed and the statement is interpreted as a trust. This is valid only if the addition is connected (wasala), not separated (fashala).
Connectivity is analogous to exception (istithna) and specification (takhsis).
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on 'qabli'
Some transmissions from Mukhtasar al-Quduri consider 'qabli' as acknowledgment of trust (amanah) because the word can cover both. However, the relied-upon view (asahh) from al-Mabsut is that it defaults to debt due to predominant usage.
The practical difference: if the confessor says only 'qabli', does he owe debt or just return a trust?
4
Apply the principle to ambiguous amounts
When a single amount is attributed to two types of property (e.g., 'I owe eleven dirhams and a dinar'), half of each is due unless each is quantified separately. This follows the same logic of splitting.
This extension shows the underlying rule of interpreting general statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله : وعلي وقبلي إقرار بدين ؛ يعني لو قال : له عليّ أو قبلي كذا، فقد أقر بالدين ؛ لأنَّ كلمته : علي للوجوب، وقبلي ينبئ عن الضمان، يقال: قبل فلان عن فلان: أي ضَمِن، والكفيل يسمى قبيلاً، والصك يسمى قبالة. كما ذكرناه في «كتاب الكفالة». وفي بعض نسخ «مختصر القُدُروي» في قوله : قبلي ؛ إنه إقرار بالأمانة ؛ لأنَّ اللفظ يتناول الأمانة والدين كليهما ، يقال : ليس لي قبل فلان دين وليس قبل فلان وديعة. وكذا إذا قال : لا حق لي قبل فلان ؛ يراد به الإبراء عن الدين والأمانة جميعاً، والأمانة أقلهما لعدم الضمان فيه فيحمل قولُ المُقِرِّ عليه ؛ لكون الأدنى هو المتيقن، والأول هو المذكور في «المبسوط»، وهو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ استعماله في الديون أغلب وأكثر فكان الحمل عليه أوجه. [2] قوله : وصدق...الخ ; يعني : وقال المقرّ في قوله : علي أو قبلي، هو وديعة، ووَصَلَ قوله : علي أو قبلي بقوله : هو وديعةً، صُدِّق ؛ لأنهما ينبئان عن الوجوب، والحفظ واجب على المودع ، والمالُ محل الحفظ، فجاز إرادة الحال عند ذكر المحلّ، كما في قولهم: نهر جار. لكنه تغيير عن أصل الوضع، فيصدِّقُ إذا وَصَلَ به، لا إن فَصَلَ عنه ؛ لأنه صار بيان تغيير، وهو يقبل إذا كان موصولاً دون مفصول، كما ترى في الاس والتخصيص وغيرهما من المغيرات.
And 'ali' and 'qabli' is an acknowledgment of debt, and he is believed if he connects it (with the statement) that it is a deposit, but not if he separates it. Because its apparent meaning is acknowledgment of debt, so his saying 'it is a deposit' becomes an explanatory change by interpreting that he is obligated to preserve the deposit, and this is valid connectedly, not disconnectedly, like exception and specification. Similarly for a dirham, and similarly for a dinar, where one is obligated from each of the two numbers for each of the two properties, and accordingly in the ambiguous: if he says 'I owe eleven dirhams and a dinar', he is obligated for half of each; if he says 'I owe eleven dirhams and eleven dinars', he is obligated for eleven of each. The principle is: whenever he mentions an amount and appends it to two types of property, half of each is obligatory, just as if he acknowledged to two men dividing it in half. Example: if he says 'I owe a hundred mithqals of gold and silver' or 'a kurr of wheat and barley', half of each is obligatory upon him. Thus in al-Tabyin and others. [1] His statement: 'And ali and qabli is an acknowledgment of debt' means if he says 'he has an obligation upon me' or 'he has a claim against me', he has acknowledged debt; because his word 'ali' indicates obligation, and 'qabli' indicates guarantee. It is said: 'qabila fulan 'an fulan' meaning he guaranteed, and the guarantor is called qabil, and the document is called qabalah. As we mentioned in the Book of Guarantee. And in some copies of Mukhtasar al-Quduri, concerning his word 'qabli', it is an acknowledgment of trust; because the word covers both trust and debt. It is said: 'I have no debt with him' and 'I have no deposit with him.' Similarly, if he says 'I have no right against him', it is meant as a release from both debt and trust. And trust is the lesser of the two because there is no guarantee in it, so the acknowledgment of the confessor is interpreted in his favor, because the lesser is certain. The first is mentioned in al-Mabsut, and it is more correct because its use in debts is more common and frequent, so interpretation upon it is more appropriate. [2] His statement: 'and he is believed... etc.' means: and the confessor says concerning his statement 'ali' or 'qabli' that it is a deposit, and he connects his statement 'ali' or 'qabli' with his statement 'it is a deposit', he is believed; because they indicate obligation, and preservation is obligatory upon the depositary, and the property is the locus of preservation, so it is permissible to intend the state (of preservation) when mentioning the locus, as in their saying 'a flowing river.' But it is a change from the original usage, so he is believed if he connects it, not if he separates it, because it becomes an explanatory change, and it is accepted when connected, not disconnected, as you see in exception and specification and other changers.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the interpretation of the words 'ali' and 'qabli' when used in acknowledgments. The author explains that these words are prima facie acknowledgments of debt (iqrar bi al-dayn). 'Ali' indicates an obligation, and 'qabli' indicates guarantee (dhaman). However, if the confessor immediately adds that it is a deposit (wadi'ah) — i.e., a trust — and does so in a connected manner (wasala), he is believed and the statement is interpreted as a trust. This is analogous to how an exception or specification (takhsis) must be connected to be valid. The author then extends the discussion to cases where the amount is ambiguous, e.g., 'I owe eleven dirhams and a dinar' — here half of each is due because the amount is split. The principle is that when a single amount is attributed to two types of property, each gets half unless each type is quantified separately. The commentary then cites a disagreement within the Hanafi school: some versions of Mukhtasar al-Quduri hold that 'qabli' is an acknowledgment of trust (amanah) because the word can cover both debt and trust, and the lesser (trust) is preferred to be cautious. However, the relied-upon position (asahh) is the one from al-Mabsut: 'qabli' is for debt because its usage in that sense is more common. The final portion discusses that if the confessor says 'ali' or 'qabli' and then, without pause, says 'it is a deposit', he is believed because he is explaining that he means the obligation to preserve, not a debt obligation. If there is a separation, the acknowledgment of debt stands and the explanation is not accepted.

Key Terms
علي (‘alay): A word indicating obligation or debt when used in acknowledgment (iqrar).
قبلي (qablī): A word indicating guarantee (dhaman) or claim against the speaker, often used for debt acknowledgment.
وديعة (wadī‘ah): A deposit of property with someone for safekeeping without liability for loss (trust).
أمانة (amānah): Trust; property held without liability for loss unless negligence occurs.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment of a right or liability against oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says 'Aliyyi kadhā' (I owe such-and-such), and immediately adds 'wa huwa wadī‘ah' (and it is a deposit), is he believed?
A) No, because he has already acknowledged debt.
B) Yes, because the addition is connected and explains the statement as a trust.
C) Only if he brings two witnesses.
D) He is not believed at all because 'ali' always means debt.

Key Takeaway
The default interpretation of 'ali' and 'qabli' is debt, and only a directly connected explanation can change it to trust.

Ruling on 'Indi' and Similar Words as Acknowledgment of Trust
Commentary on the matn that words like 'indi' indicate trust, not debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that locative expressions in acknowledgments indicate trust, and explain the two proofs (physical implication and custom) supporting this interpretation.

Core Idea
When someone says 'with me,' it is a trust not a debt—because words of place refer to physical objects, not liabilities.

Imagine someone acknowledges, 'Your book is with me.' Is that a loan or a trust? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer depends on the words used. This lesson examines a key rule: words like 'with me' ('indi) and 'in my house' indicate that the property is held as a trust (amanah), not a debt. The reasoning is based on the nature of physical space and customary usage.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the locative words
Identify the key expressions: 'indi (with me), ma'i (accompanying me), fi bayti (in my house), fi kisi (in my bag), fi sunduqi (in my chest). These are all locative phrases indicating physical possession.
These are the exact words from the matn.
2
Understand the physical vs. abstract distinction
Locative words refer to a physical place. Debt (dayn) is an abstract liability that resides in the dhimmah (legal obligation), not in a physical space. Therefore, these words cannot refer to debt; they must refer to tangible property ('ayn).
The commentary explicitly states that the locus of debt is the dhimmah.
3
Apply the presumption of trust
Tangible property can be held as either a guarantee (e.g., loan) or a trust (amanah). The default is trust, because possession implies safekeeping unless guarantee is explicitly stated or proven.
Trust is more likely, so the acknowledgment is interpreted as trust.
4
Consider the role of custom ('urf)
Even apart from the physical reasoning, these words are customarily used for trusts. The Hanafi principle is that absolute speech is interpreted according to custom (al-'urf). This strengthens the conclusion.
Al-Manh and other works explicitly state this custom-based reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندي، أو معي، أو في بيتي، أو في كيسي، أو في صندوقي أمانة.
[1] قوله : وعندي...الخ ; يعني ولو قال: عندي، أو قال : معي، أو قال: في بيتي أو قال: في كيسي، فهو إقرار بأمانة، لأنَّ هذه المواضع محل للعين لا الدين، إذ محلّه الذمة، والعين يحتمل أن تكون مضمونة أمانة، والأمانة أولى فيحمل عليها. وهذا لأنَّ كلمة عند للظرف، ومع للقرآن وما عداهما لمكان معين، فيكون من خصائص العين، ولا يحتملُ الدِّينُ لاستحالة كونه في هذه الأماكن، فإذا كانت من خصائص العين تعينت الأمانة لما ذكرنا ; ولأنَّ هذه الكلمات في العرف والعادات تستعمل في الأمانات ومطلق الكلام يحمل على العرف، صرح به في «المنح ) ، وغيره.
And 'with me' or 'with me' or 'in my house' or 'in my bag' or 'in my chest' is a trust. [1] His statement: 'And 'indi... etc.' means: if he says 'with me' or says 'with me' or says 'in my house' or says 'in my bag', it is an acknowledgment of trust, because these places are loci for tangible property (al-'ayn) not debt, since the locus of debt is the dhimmah (liability). Tangible property may be either guaranteed (like loan) or trust, and trust is more likely, so it is interpreted accordingly. This is because the word 'inda is for container, and 'ma'a' for accompaniment, and the others for a specific place, so they are characteristics of 'ayn, and debt cannot possibly be in these places. Since they are characteristics of 'ayn, trust is determined as we mentioned; and because these words are used in custom and habit for trusts, and absolute speech is interpreted according to custom, as stated in al-Manh and others.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that states if a person says 'with me' ('indi), 'with me' (ma'i), 'in my house', 'in my bag', or 'in my chest', it is an acknowledgment of trust (amanah), not debt. The reason is that these locative expressions refer to physical space, which can only contain tangible property ('ayn), not debt. Debt resides in the dhimmah (abstract liability), not in a physical place. Tangible property could be either a guaranteed liability (like a loan) or a trust (amanah). Since trust is the default and more likely (because possession implies trust unless proven otherwise), the acknowledgment is interpreted as trust. The commentary emphasizes that these words are customarily used for trusts, and the general principle is that speech is interpreted according to custom ('urf). This is explicitly stated in the work al-Manh. The author also notes that 'inda indicates a container/place, and ma'a indicates accompaniment, and the others indicate a specific location—all are characteristics of physical objects, not debts. Therefore, the acknowledgment is for a trust, not a debt.

Key Terms
عندي (‘indī): A locative word meaning 'with me' or 'in my possession', typically indicating physical possession of property (trust).
أمانة (amānah): Trust; property held without liability for loss except in case of negligence.
عرف (‘urf): Custom; established usage that determines the interpretation of speech in legal contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Acknowledgment of a loan (qard).
B) Acknowledgment of a trust (amanah).
C) Acknowledgment of a gift (hibah).
D) Not an acknowledgment at all.

Key Takeaway
Locative expressions like 'indi' in acknowledgments are interpreted as trust, not debt, based on physical implication and customary usage.

Imperative with Object Pronoun as Acknowledgment
Commentary specifying the condition for an imperative to be acknowledgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which an imperative command to weigh or count constitutes a valid acknowledgment (iqrar) in Hanafi fiqh, and apply that condition to a given scenario.

Core Idea
An imperative without an object pronoun does not create an acknowledgment—the pronoun 'ha' specifies liability.

In the matn of al-Wiqayah, the author discusses the phrase 'Whoever claims a thousand, let him weigh it.' The commentator explains that this phrase is an acknowledgment because the imperative 'weigh it' includes the feminine pronoun 'ha', which refers to the dirhams. Without that pronoun—if one simply says 'weigh' or 'count'—there is no acknowledgment. This distinction hinges on grammatical specificity and shows how Hanafi fiqh treats indirect expressions as binding when they clearly imply liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verbal act
The person is uttering a command (imperative) to a claimant regarding a claimed amount. Determine the exact wording: does it include a direct object pronoun or not?
The example given is 'atzinha' (weigh it) vs. 'atzin' (weigh) without 'ha'.
2
Check for the direct object pronoun
Look for the attached pronoun 'ha' (or 'him' for masculine objects) attached to the imperative verb. This pronoun indicates what the command is directed towards, specifying the object.
In the text, 'atzinha' has the feminine 'ha' because dirhams (feminine) are understood.
3
Classify the ruling
If the pronoun is present, the statement is an acknowledgment (iqrar) and binding; the speaker is held to owe the amount. If absent, it is not an acknowledgment.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the grammatical basis
The pronoun makes the verb transitive to a specific object. Without it, the command is general and does not indicate an obligation of the speaker.
Hanafi usul holds that ambiguous expressions that clearly imply liability are sufficient for iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله لمدعي الألف: اتزنها
[2] قوله : مدعي الألف ; يعني إنَّ رجلاً لو قال : من ادعى ألفاً اتزنها، أو انتقدها ونحو ذلك مما هو المذكور في المتن فيكون هذا إقرار، وإن قال : له اتزن أو انتقد ونحو ذلك بلا ذكر الضمير، فلا يكون إقراراً.
[3] قوله : اتزنها ; أمر معناه خذ بالوزن الواجب لك على أصله، أو اتزنها قلب الواو تاء ، وأدغمت في التاء، وإنّما أَنَّث الضمير مع أنَّ الألـف مـن العـدد اعتباراً للدراهم.
And his statement concerning the claimant of a thousand: 'weigh it.' [2] His statement: 'claimant of a thousand'; means if a man says: 'Whoever claims a thousand, let him weigh it (atzinha) or count it (intaqidha)', or similar to what is mentioned in the matn, then this is an acknowledgment. But if he says: 'let him weigh' or 'let him count' without mentioning the pronoun (ha), it is not an acknowledgment. [3] His statement: 'atzinha'; a command meaning 'take by the weight that is obligatory for you upon his origin', or 'atzinha' is from the root wazana, with waw changed to ta' and assimilated. The pronoun is feminine (ha) even though 'alf' is masculine, because it refers to dirhams (feminine).
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn in which the author discusses the phrase 'whoever claims a thousand, let him weigh it' (man idda‘a alfan atzinha). The commentator explains that this statement constitutes an acknowledgment (iqrar) of the debt, because the imperative 'weigh it' implies that the speaker owes that amount and is directing the claimant to take what is owed. However, if the speaker says 'let him weigh' or 'let him count' without the attached pronoun 'ha' (i.e., without specifying the object), it is not an acknowledgment because it lacks specificity. The commentator also provides a grammatical note: the verb 'atzinha' is derived from wazana (to weigh), with the waw becoming ta' and assimilated. The feminine pronoun 'ha' is used even though 'alf' (thousand) is masculine, because it refers to dirhams (which are feminine) underlyingly. This illustrates that in legal acknowledgments, even indirect expressions can be binding if they clearly imply liability.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment; here, an utterance that implies the speaker owes a right to another.
اتزنها (atzinahā): Imperative verb meaning 'weigh it' (the thousand-amount), implying the speaker owes it.
الضمير (aḍ-ḍamīr): The attached pronoun (ha) that makes the verb transitive and specifies the object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person says to a claimant: 'Iznī' (weigh for me). According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, is this an acknowledgment?
A) Yes, because 'weigh for me' implies the speaker owes something.
B) No, because the imperative lacks an attached pronoun specifying the claim.
C) Yes, if the amount was previously mentioned.
D) No, because weighing is not necessarily related to debt.

Key Takeaway
An imperative command to take or weigh is only an acknowledgment of debt if it includes a direct object pronoun specifying the claimed amount.

Interpretation of 'Indi, Ma'i as Acknowledgment of Trust
Commentary defining location phrases as acknowledgment of trust

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that phrases like 'indi and ma'i constitute acknowledgment of trust and explain the linguistic and customary basis for this Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
Phrases indicating physical location imply trust, not debt, because debts are abstract.

When someone says 'it is with me' or similar, what does that mean legally? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi interpretation that such phrases are acknowledgments of a trust (amanah), not a debt. We will examine the linguistic reasoning and the role of custom (urf) in this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases and the legal issue
The phrases are 'indi, ma'i, fi bayti, fi kisi, fi sunduqi. The issue: do they acknowledge trust or debt?
2
Analyze the linguistic implication
These words denote physical location (container, companionship), thus only applicable to tangible objects (ayn), not abstract debts.
The word 'inda indicates a container; ma'a indicates companionship.
3
Apply the default presumption
Tangible objects held by another are presumed trust (amanah) unless proven otherwise (e.g., guarantee).
The default for physical custody is amanah.
4
Consider customary usage
In common speech, these phrases are used for trusts; absolute speech is interpreted according to custom (urf).
Urf is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh.
5
State the ruling
Therefore, the statement is an acknowledgment of trust (iqrar bi-amanah), binding the speaker to return the trust.
The hukm level is wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندي، أو معي، أو في بيتي، أو في كيسي، أو في صندوقي أمانة. قال الشارح: يعني ولو قال: عندي، أو قال: معي، أو قال: في بيتي، أو قال: في كيسي، فهو إقرار بأمانة؛ لأن هذه المواضع محل للعين لا الدين، إذ محل الدين الذمة، والعين يحتمل أن تكون مضمونة أو أمانة، والأمانة أولى فتحمل عليها. وذلك لأن كلمة عند للظرف، ومع للمصاحبة، وما عداهما لمكان معين، فيكون من خصائص العين، ولا يحتمل الدين لاستحالة كونه في هذه الأماكن. فإذا كانت من خصائص العين تعينت الأمانة. ولأن هذه الكلمات في العرف والعادات تستعمل في الأمانات، ومطلق الكلام يحمل على العرف.
And 'with me', or 'with me', or 'in my house', or 'in my purse', or 'in my chest as a trust'. The commentator said: meaning if he said 'with me', or said 'with me', or said 'in my house', or said 'in my purse', it is an acknowledgment of a trust; because these places are a locus for a tangible object not a debt, for the locus of debt is the liability, and the tangible object may be either guaranteed or a trust, and the trust is more likely so it is interpreted accordingly. And that is because the word 'inda indicates a container, and ma'a indicates companionship, and other than these for a specific place, so it is among the specific characteristics of a tangible object, and debt is not possible because it is inconceivable for it to be in these places. So when they are specific to tangible objects, trust is determined. And because these words in custom and habits are used for trusts, and absolute speech is interpreted according to custom.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage defines a legal rule: when someone says 'it is with me' or similar, it is interpreted as an acknowledgment of a trust (amanah), not a debt. The commentator explains that these phrases denote physical location, which is only possible for tangible objects, not debts (since debts are abstract). Therefore, the object must be a physical item, and the default for physical items held by another is trust (amanah) rather than guarantee (damān), unless proven otherwise. The reasoning combines linguistic analysis (these words imply a container or companionship, specific to tangible objects) and customary usage (in common speech, such phrases refer to items held in trust). This is a Hanafi position based on urf (custom) and linguistic principles.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or obligation; here specifically admission of holding a trust.
أمانة (amanah): Item held in trust, not subject to guarantee unless negligence occurs.
عرف (urf): Customary usage that determines interpretation of ambiguous statements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets locative phrases like 'indī and ma'ī as acknowledgments of trust (amanah) based on linguistic analysis and customary usage.

Effect of Pronoun and Mockery in Acknowledgment
Commentary on iqrar via command with pronoun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a pronoun in a response to a claim creates binding acknowledgment, and distinguish when mockery negates it.

Core Idea
A pronoun ties the response to the claim; its absence leaves ambiguity, no acknowledgment.

In Hanafi iqrar, the exact wording matters. This passage explains how a simple pronoun can turn a command into a binding admission of debt. We will trace the linguistic logic and the exception for proven mockery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrases
Examine the phrases listed: 'atiznaha', 'intaqidha', etc. Note the common feature: the pronoun 'ha' attached to the verb.
The pronoun refers to the claimed amount (thousand dirhams).
2
Understand the role of the pronoun
The author explains that the pronoun acts as a reference (kinayah) back to the claim, making the response equivalent to repeating the claim. This creates an acknowledgment.
Without the pronoun, the words are ambiguous—'weigh your speech' not 'weigh the money'.
3
State the ruling for each scenario
With pronoun: iqrar (binding acknowledgment). Without pronoun: no iqrar. The onus is on the claimant to prove otherwise.
The principle: the response must be coherent and refer back to the claim to be an acknowledgment.
4
Analyze the exception for mockery
If the defendant's statement was made mockingly and witnesses testify to that, it is not binding. But bare claim of mockery is not accepted without evidence.
Analogous to the divorce case: a man who says 'you are divorced' and later claims he meant 'divorce from a shackle' is not believed.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a new phrase like 'انتقدها' (intaqidha) with pronoun, it is iqrar. If 'انتقد' (intaqid) without pronoun, it is not.
The illa (effective cause) is the presence of a pronoun explicitly referring to the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله مدعي الألف: يعني إن رجلاً لو قال: من ادعى ألفاً اتزنها، أو انتقدها، أو أجلني بها، أو قضيتكها، أو أبراتني منها، أو تصدقت بها علي، أو وهبتها لي، أو أحلتك بها على زيد، فهو إقرار. وإن قال: له اتزن أو انتقد ونحو ذلك بلا ذكر الضمير، فلا يكون إقراراً. لأن الهاء كناية عن المذكور في الدعوى، فصار كأنه أعاد المدعي وهو الألف، فيكون إقراراً بها. بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن فيه ضمير يرجع إلى الألف؛ لأنه لا دليل على انصرافه إلى المذكور، فيكون كلاماً مبتدأ ويراد: زن كلامك بميزان العقل، أو انتقد كلامك، ونحو ذلك، فلا يلزمه شيء. والأصل أن الجواب ينتظم إعادة الخطاب ليفيد الكلام، فكل ما يصلح لذلك فهو إقرار. وقوله: إقرار؛ هذا إذا لم يكن على سبيل الاستهزاء. أما إذا كان على سبيل الاستهزاء، وشهد الشهود بذلك، لم يلزمه شيء. أما إذا ادعى أنه قال مستهزئاً لم يقبل منه، كما من طلق امرأته وقال: عنيت طلاقاً من وثاق.
His statement 'He means the one claiming a thousand': meaning if a man said: whoever claims a thousand, weigh it (atiznaha), or criticize it (intaqidha), or give me time with it (ajilnī biha), or I have settled it (qadaytukaha), or you have acquitted me of it (abra'tanī minha), or you gave it in charity to me (tasaddaqta biha 'alayya), or you gifted it to me (wahibta lī), or I have transferred it to you on Zaid (ahaltuka biha 'alā Zaid), it is an acknowledgment. And if he said: to him it is weight (atizin) or criticize (intaqid) and so on without mentioning the pronoun, then it is not an acknowledgment. Because the pronoun (hā') is a reference to the mentioned matter in the claim, so it becomes as if he repeated the claimant which is the thousand, thus it is an acknowledgment of it. Contrary to when there is no pronoun referring to the thousand; because there is no evidence that it refers to what was mentioned, so it is an independent statement intended to mean: weigh your speech with the scale of reason, or criticize your speech, and so on, thus nothing is binding on him. And the principle is that the response must include repeating the address to benefit the speech, so everything that does that is an acknowledgment. And his statement: 'acknowledgment' – this is if not in a mocking manner. If it was in a mocking manner, and witnesses testified to that, nothing is binding on him. But if he claims he said it mockingly without witnesses, it is not accepted from him, like the one who divorced his wife and said 'I meant a divorce from a shackle.'
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific scenario in iqrar (acknowledgment). The base text states: if a man says 'whoever claims a thousand dirhams, weigh it (atiznaha)' and similar phrases like 'criticize it' or 'give me time with it' etc., using a pronoun (the 'ha' suffix) referring to the claimed amount, this constitutes an acknowledgment (iqrar) of the debt. But if he omits the pronoun and says 'weigh' (atizin) without reference, it is not an acknowledgment. The commentator explains the legal reasoning: the pronoun ties the statement back to the claim, effectively confirming the debt. Without the pronoun, the words can be interpreted as general advice or rhetoric (like 'weigh your speech'). The passage also includes an exception: if the statement was made mockingly (istihza') and witnesses testify to that, it is not binding. However, if the person claims it was mockery without evidence, his claim is not accepted, analogous to a husband who divorces and then claims he meant 'divorce from a shackle' (i.e., a metaphor). This shows that iqrar is generally binding unless there is clear proof of jest.

Key Terms
ضمير (damir): The pronoun (ha') attached to the verb, referring to the claimed amount.
استهزاء (istihza'): Mockery or jest, which when proven by witnesses, negates the binding effect of iqrar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a defendant says to the claimant 'اتزن' (weigh) without a pronoun, according to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) It is an acknowledgment.
B) It is NOT an acknowledgment.
C) It is an acknowledgment only if the claimant is present.
D) It is an acknowledgment if the amount is stated separately.

Key Takeaway
A pronoun in a response to a claim creates binding acknowledgment; without it, no acknowledgment. Proven mockery is an exception.

Acknowledgment of Deferred Debt: Creditor's Claim of Immediacy
Explains Hanafi and Shafi'i/Ahmad positions on creditor's claim of immediacy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the acknowledgment of a deferred debt when the creditor claims it is immediate, and compare it with the Shafi'i/Ahmad position, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the creditor's oath converts a deferred debt into an immediate one—the debtor's acknowledgment binds him to the debt, not its terms.

When a debtor acknowledges a debt but claims it is deferred, and the creditor insists it is immediate, which statement is accepted? The Hanafi school holds that the debtor's acknowledgment establishes the debt itself, while the term (deferral) is a separate condition that the debtor must prove. Since the creditor denies the deferral, he is considered the denier and his oath prevails, making the debt due immediately. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with the Shafi'i/Ahmad position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules: the creditor is believed with oath, and the debt becomes due immediately.
The debtor's acknowledgment is proof against himself for the debt, but the deferral is an additional claim by the debtor.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
State which school(s) object (Shafi'i and Ahmad) and their reasoning: the acknowledgment itself includes the deferral, so the debtor's own words indicate it is deferred; the creditor must prove otherwise.
Some Malikis also hold a similar view.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Explain how the Hanafis rebut: the acknowledgment establishes the debt, not the terms; terms are additional claims. The debtor claims deferral, so he must prove it; the creditor denies it, so his oath suffices.
This is based on the principle that al-aṣl fī al-ʿawāriḍ al-inkār.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Articulate the practical difference: In Hanafi fiqh, the creditor can immediately demand payment upon oath; in Shafi'i fiqh, the debtor benefits from the deferral unless the creditor provides proof.
This difference affects the creditor's immediate right to enforce the debt.

وإن أقر بدين مؤجلٍ صُدِّقَ المقر له إن قال : هو حال وحلف به (وإن أقر بدين مؤجل صُدِّقَ المقر له إن قال : هو حال وحلف به ) : أي حلف المقر له على أنه ليس بمؤجل، فيجب له الدِّينُ حالاً. جواباً ولا يصلح ابتداءً يجعلُ، وما يصلح للابتداء لا للبناء أو يصلح لهما فإنه يجعل ابتداء ; لوقوع الشك في كونه جواباً ، فلا يجعل جواباً ; لئلا يلزمه المال بالشك، فإن ذكر الضمير يصلح جواباً لا ابتداء، وإن لم يذكره لا يصلح جواباً أو يصلح ابتداء وجواباً فلا يكون إقراراً بالشك، وهذا إذا كان الجواب مستقلاً. وإن كان غير مستقل كقوله : نعم ; يكون إقراراً مطلقاً; لأنه غير مستقل ، كقوله : نعم، يكون إقراراً مطلقاً ; لأنه غير مستقل، وقد أخرجه جواباً، وهو صالح له، فصار ما تقدم من الخطاب كالمعاوضة فيه. صرح به الزيلعي) ، وغيره. وقال الشافعي وأحمد الله في اتَّزنَ وانتقد : إنه ليس بإقرار وبه قال بعض أصحاب مالك له ، لأنه يحتمل الإقرار، ويحتمل الاستهزاء، والمبالغة في الحجود، فلا يكون إقراراً بالشك، ذكره السمني في «كمال الدراية». قوله : وإن أقر...الخ ; يعني إنّ رجلاً لو أقر بدين مؤجل، وقال المقر له : هو حال، صُدق المقر له، وحلف على الأجل ; لكونه منكرا، ويجب على المقر ذلك حالاً ; لأنه أقر بحق على نفسه، وادّعى حقاً على المقر له ، فإقراره في حقه حجة ، وفي حق غيره دعوى ، ولا تقبل دعواه بلا حجة. وعند الشافعي له وأحمد الله لزمه مؤجّلاً مع يمينه. ذكره العيني في «شرح الكنز». بخلاف ما لو أقرّ بالدراهم السود فكذَّبه في صفتها، حيث يلزمه : أي المقرّ ما أقر به فقط، ولا يقبل قول المقرّ له فيه ; لأنّ السود نوع من الدراهم، والقولُ قولُ المقر في النوع، والأجل لا يثبت بنفس العقد، بل بالشرط. فالقول قول المنكر في العوارض كإقرار الكفيل بدين مؤجل ; فإنه يكون القول في الأجل دون المقر له ; لأنّ الأجل في الكفالة يثبت فيهما من غير شرط، بأن كفل ديناً مؤجلاً. كذا في «المنح»)، وغيره.
And if he acknowledges a deferred debt, the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made is believed if he says it is immediate and swears upon it. (And if he acknowledges a deferred debt, the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made is believed if he says it is immediate and swears upon it): Meaning, the one in whose favor the acknowledgment is made swears that it is not deferred, so the debt becomes due immediately. (Then commentary about the utterance being a response or initiating, etc.) And al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said concerning 'he weighed and paid' that it is not an acknowledgment, and some of Malik's companions said the same, because it could be an acknowledgment or mockery and exaggeration in denial, so it is not an acknowledgment due to doubt. Al-Sumunni mentioned it in Kamal al-Diraya. 'His saying: And if he acknowledges...etc.' meaning: If a man acknowledges a deferred debt, and the beneficiary says it is immediate, the beneficiary is believed and he swears concerning the term because he is denying, and the debt becomes due immediately because he acknowledged a right against himself and claimed a right against the beneficiary, so his acknowledgment is proof regarding himself, and a claim regarding others, and his claim is not accepted without proof. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it remains deferred with his oath. Al-Ayni mentioned it in Sharh al-Kanz. Contrary to the case where he acknowledges black dirhams and then contradicts their description, where only what he acknowledged is binding and the beneficiary's statement is not accepted because 'black' is a type of dirham, and the statement is that of the acknowledger regarding the type, and the term is not established by the contract itself but by condition. So the statement of the denier is accepted regarding contingencies, like the acknowledgment of a guarantor of a deferred debt; then the statement is regarding the term, not the beneficiary, because the term in guarantee is established in both without a condition, as in guaranteeing a deferred debt. Thus in al-Manh, etc.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the acknowledgment of a deferred debt. The Hanafi position is that if a debtor acknowledges a delayed debt, but the creditor claims it is immediate and takes an oath, the creditor is believed and the debt becomes immediately due. The reasoning is that the debtor's acknowledgment is binding on himself, so it establishes the debt. The creditor's claim of immediacy is a denial of the deferral, and since deferral is an additional condition that the debtor asserts, the burden of proof lies on him. The debtor cannot prove it, so the creditor’s oath is accepted. The Shafi'i and Ahmad's positions are that the debt remains deferred because the acknowledgment itself includes the delay; the debtor's own words indicate it is deferred, so the creditor must prove otherwise. The Hanafis counter that the acknowledgment establishes the debt but any condition of delay is a separate claim. The text also discusses a nuance: if the acknowledgment is in response to a question and the utterance can only be a response, it is valid. If it could be both a response or an initiation, it is not an acknowledgment due to doubt. The example of acknowledging black dirhams shows that if the type is disputed, the debtor's statement about the type is accepted because it is a detail within the acknowledged item. However, the term (delay) is not part of the debt's essence but a separate condition, so the creditor's denial (that it is delayed) is accepted.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment of a right or obligation by a person against himself.
مقر له (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made (the creditor/beneficiary).
مؤجل (mu'ajjal): A debt or obligation that is deferred to a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a debtor acknowledges a deferred debt, what must the creditor do to claim it is immediate?
A) Provide witnesses to the lack of deferral
B) Take an oath that it is not deferred
C) Produce a written contract
D) Accept the debtor's deferral

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an acknowledgment of a deferred debt does not prove the deferral; the creditor can claim immediacy and his oath makes the debt due immediately.

Hanafi Istihsan in Interpreting Ambiguous Numbers in Iqrār
Explains Hanafi istihsan and Shafi'i qiyas in interpreting ambiguous number expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a measured or weighed item following an ambiguous number in an iqrar specifies that number, and distinguish this from the Shafi'i qiyas.

Core Idea
A measured or weighed item after an ambiguous number specifies it; other items do not.

When someone acknowledges a debt with an ambiguous number like 'a hundred and a dirham', should the dirham specify the hundred? The Shafi'i school applies strict qiyas, leaving the hundred ambiguous. The Hanafi school uses istihsan based on customary usage, treating dirhams and other measurable items as natural discriminators. This lesson unpacks the istihsan reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed expression
Look at the iqrar 'a hundred and a dirham'. The number 'a hundred' is ambiguous without a unit. The word 'dirham' is coordinated with 'and'. The dispute is whether 'dirham' clarifies the hundred.
2
State the Shafi'i qiyas ruling
By qiyas, the hundred remains ambiguous because 'dirham' is a coordinated item, like 'a hundred and a garment' where the garment does not specify the hundred. The debtor must clarify the hundred later.
The base case for qiyas is 'a hundred and a garment' – there is consensus that this does not specify the hundred.
3
Explain the Hanafi istihsan
Hanafis deviate from qiyas via istihsan: when the coordinated item is a measured or weighed item (e.g., dirham, wheat), it naturally serves as a discriminator for the ambiguous number. Thus 'a hundred and a dirham' means one hundred dirhams plus one extra dirham.
The illa in qiyas is incomplete – it ignores the difference between measured/weighed items and counted items.
4
Analyze the distinction: measured/weighed vs counted
Apply the distinction: 'a hundred and a garment' – garment is a counted item, so it does not specify (consensus). 'A hundred and a dirham' – dirham is a weighed item, so it specifies. Also, if two ambiguous numbers appear without 'wa' before the discriminator (e.g., 'a hundred three garments'), the discriminator applies to both.
Hanafis use 'iktifa' (sufficiency) for the latter case: the discriminator suffices for both numbers.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the debtor owes a specified amount (e.g., 101 dirhams). Under Shafi'i law, the hundred remains unspecified, and the debtor must later clarify, leading to potential disputes.
The Hanafi ruling is mufta bihi due to istihsan overriding qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومئة ودرهم كلها دراهم، وفي مئة وثوب، ومئة وثوبان تفسر المئة، ومئة وثلاثة أثواب كلها ثياب. اعلم أن في قوله : لفلان علي مئة ودرهم عند الشافعي تفسر المئة كما في علي مئة وثوب، وهو القياس، وعندنا : إذا ذكر بعد لفظ العدد ما هو من المقدرات قوله في الأجل دون المقر له ; لأنّ الأجل في الكفالة يثبت فيهما من غير شرط، بأن كفل ديناً مؤجلاً. كذا في «المنح»)، وغيره. [1] قوله : ومئة ودرهم...الخ ; يعني لو قال : له علي مئة ودرهم، فالكل دراهم، ويلزمه مئة درهم، ودرهم واحد استحساناً عندنا ; لوقوع درهم تفسيراً للمئة المبهمة ، والقياس أن يرجع في تفسير المئة إليه، وهو قول الشافعي له. وكذا كل ما يكال أو يوزن، فلو قال: له على مئة وقفيزُ حنطة، يلزمه مئة قفيز ، وقفيز حنطة، وإن قال له عليَّ مئة وثوب أو قال له علي مئة وثوبان، لزمه تفسير المئة، فيلزمُهُ ثوب واحد في الصورة الأولى، وثوبان في الصورة الثانية بالاتفاق ; لأنّ المئة مبهمة، والثوب عطف عليها لا تفسير لها ; لأنَّ المعطوف لم يوضع لتفسير المعطوف عليه، ولم يكن من قبيل الاكتفاء. كما في مئة ودرهم، وإن قال : له علي مئة وثلاثة أثواب فالكل ثياب، فيلزمها أثواب في الكلّ ; لأنه ذكر عددين مبهمين، وذكر عقبهما مميزاً بلا واو، فينصرفُ إليهما ; لاستوائهما في الحاجة إلى التفسير لعدد واحد بالاقتران. [٢] قوله : وهو القياس ; حاصله : إنَّ القياس هو أن يرجع في تفسير المئة إليه، في قوله : مئة ودرهم كما في قوله : مئة وثوب أو مائة وثوبان ، وهو قول الشافعي الله : لأنَّ عطف مفسراً على مبهم في الفصلين، والمعطوف غير المعطوف عليه، ولم يوضع للبيان، فبقيت المئة على إبهامها كما في عطف الثوب عليها. [٣] قوله : وعندنا...الخ ; هذا هو الاستحسان ، وتقريره : إنَّ عطف الموزون (ناقص)
And a hundred and a dirham, all are dirhams. And in a hundred and a garment, and a hundred and two garments, the hundred is interpreted. And a hundred and three garments, all are garments. Know that in his saying: 'For so-and-so upon me a hundred and a dirham' according to al-Shafi'i, the hundred is interpreted as in 'upon me a hundred and a garment', and that is the analogy (qiyas). According to us (Hanafis): When he mentions after the number what is of measured items... his saying regarding the term, not the beneficiary, because the term in guarantee is established in both without a condition, as in guaranteeing a deferred debt. Thus in al-Manh, etc. [1] His saying: 'And a hundred and a dirham...etc.' meaning if he says: 'Upon me a hundred and a dirham', then all are dirhams, and he is obliged to pay a hundred dirhams and one dirham by istihsan according to us, because the dirham occurs as an interpretation of the ambiguous hundred, and the analogy (qiyas) would be that the interpretation of the hundred is referred back to him, which is the saying of al-Shafi'i. Likewise for everything that is measured or weighed, if he says: 'Upon me a hundred and a qafiz of wheat', he is obliged to pay a hundred qafiz and a qafiz of wheat. If he says: 'Upon me a hundred and a garment' or 'a hundred and two garments', the hundred is interpreted, so he is obliged one garment in the first case and two garments in the second case by consensus, because the hundred is ambiguous, and the garment is coordinated with it, not an interpretation of it, because the coordinated is not placed to interpret the coordinated-upon, and it is not of the type of sufficiency (iktifa'). As in 'a hundred and a dirham'. And if he says: 'Upon me a hundred and three garments', then all are garments, and he is obliged garments in all, because he mentioned two ambiguous numbers and mentioned after them a discriminator without the conjunction 'wa', so it refers to both because they are equal in needing interpretation for a single number by conjunction. [2] His saying: 'And it is the analogy' – its summary: The analogy is that the interpretation of the hundred is referred back to him in his saying 'a hundred and a dirham' as in 'a hundred and a garment' or 'a hundred and two garments', and that is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because coordinating a specific item to an ambiguous item in both phrases, and the coordinated is not the coordinated-upon, and it is not placed for clarification, so the hundred remains ambiguous as in coordinating a garment to it. [3] His saying: 'And according to us...etc.' – this is the istihsan, and its explanation: The coordination of a weighed item... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage addresses the interpretation of ambiguous number expressions in acknowledgment, specifically when someone acknowledges 'a hundred and a dirham' or similar. The Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas. According to istihsan, when a word denoting a measured or weighed item (like dirham or qafiz) follows an ambiguous number connected by 'wa' (and), that word specifies the preceding number, so 'a hundred and a dirham' means one hundred dirhams plus one extra dirham. This is because the measured/weighed item is naturally used to interpret the ambiguous number. The Shafi'i school follows qiyas: the number remains ambiguous and must be clarified by the debtor, as in 'a hundred and a garment' where the garment does not interpret the hundred. The Hanafis distinguish between measured/weighed items (which can be used as discriminators) and counted items (like garments). The text also gives examples: with 'a hundred and a garment' or 'a hundred and two garments', the hundred is interpreted separately by consensus because the garment is not a discriminator. If two ambiguous numbers are given without 'wa' between them and the subsequent item and a discriminator without 'wa', like 'a hundred and three garments' (actually 'a hundred three garments' without 'and' before three?), the text says all are garments because the last word clarifies both numbers. The passage then explains the qiyas reasoning of Shafi'i and then states that the Hanafi position is istihsan, but the text is cut off before fully explaining the istihsan reasoning.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas for a stronger reason, often based on custom, necessity, or equity.
تفسير (tafsir): Interpretation or specification of an ambiguous expression.
مبهم (mubham): Ambiguous or unspecified term, e.g., the number 'hundred' without a unit.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction from a primary case to a new case based on a common effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The debt is invalid due to ambiguity

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iqrar, a measured or weighed item following an ambiguous number specifies it; other items do not.
