Acknowledgment of a Beast in a Stable
Hanafi ruling on acknowledgment of a beast in a stable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledging a beast in a stable and explain why the stable is not included.

Core Idea
Acknowledging a beast in a stable only admits ownership of the beast, not the stable, because immovable property cannot be a subordinate.

This lesson deals with a specific case in iqrar (acknowledgment). When someone says 'I have a beast in the stable,' does that acknowledge the beast only or also the stable? The Hanafi school, based on the reasoning of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, rules that only the beast is binding. The stable, being immovable, cannot be considered a subordinate or subject to the same legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is the legal act being considered? It is the acknowledgment (iqrar) of a beast that is in a stable.
Acknowledgment is a legal statement admitting ownership or liability.
2
Determine the ruling
According to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, only the beast is binding (lazim), not the stable.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The stable is immovable property. Usurpation (ghasb) of the stable is not possible because it cannot be physically transferred. Therefore, the stable cannot be considered a subordinate (tabi') of the beast in acknowledgment.
Immovables and movables are treated differently in Hanafi iqrar.
4
Note the authority
The ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad al-Shaybani may have a different view, but the text does not specify it.
The author of the text follows the majority of the early Hanafi masters.
5
Recognize the implication
In practice, acknowledging an item located on real property does not automatically include the property itself.
This distinction prevents unintended legal obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإقرار بدابة) : يعني لو أقر بدابة في اصطبل لزمه الدابة فقط ، ولا يلزمه الاصطبل عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ؛ لأن غصب الاصطبل لا يتحقق لعدم إمكان النقل ؛ لكونه محلاً للغير، فلا يكون الاصطبل تابعاً للدابة.
And acknowledgment of a beast: meaning if he acknowledged a beast in a stable, only the beast is binding upon him, and the stable is not binding upon him according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, because usurpation of the stable is not realized due to impossibility of transfer, since it is a place for others, so the stable is not a subordinate of the beast.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: if someone acknowledges that he has a beast (such as a horse or camel) inside a stable, only the beast is considered acknowledged, not the stable itself. The reasoning is that the stable is real property (immovable) and cannot be usurped or taken by transfer in the same way movable property can. Therefore, the stable does not follow the beast in the acknowledgment. This is the position of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
اصطبل (isṭabl): A stable, a place where beasts are kept.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; illegal taking of property. Here, the stable cannot be usurped because it is immovable.
دابة (dābba): A beast, such as a horse or camel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, acknowledging a beast in a stable does not imply ownership of the stable because immovable property cannot be considered a subordinate in acknowledgment.

Scope of Confession: Beast in Stable and Ring
Determines scope of confession for beast in stable and ring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that confession to 'a beast in a stable' binds only the beast, and to 'a ring' binds both the band and the stone, and distinguish the disputed position of Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the stable.

Core Idea
When two items are mentioned, inclusion depends on whether one is a follower (tābiʿ) of the other.

In this lesson, we examine two confession scenarios: 'a beast in a stable' and 'a ring.' The first involves a dispute between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad on whether the stable is included. The second is unanimously agreed: the linguistic scope of 'ring' (khātam) covers both the band and the stone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and locate the two distinct cases: (1) confession to 'a beast in a stable,' (2) confession to 'a ring.' Note the ruling for each.
The stable case has a dispute; the ring case has consensus among the Hanafi imams.
2
Analyze the stable case
Understand why only the beast is binding according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf: the stable is real estate, its transfer is impossible, so it cannot be a follower (tābiʿ) of the beast.
Imam Muhammad’s opposing view is that usurpation of real estate is possible, so both are binding.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: if someone confesses to 'a beast in a stable,' according to the majority (two shaykhs) only the beast is owed; according to Muhammad, both beast and stable are owed.
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf.
4
Examine the ring case
Note that there is no dispute here: the name 'ring' includes the band (ḥalqah) and stone (faṣṣ) linguistically, so confessing to a ring binds both parts.
This is supported by the principle that the stone is included in the sale of a ring without specification.
5
Synthesize the principle
Formulate the general rule: in confessions and sales, the scope of a word includes its integral parts (like the stone of a ring) but not independent adjuncts (like a stable for a beast) unless they are deemed followers by usage or necessity.
This distinction is based on the nature of the items: movable vs. immovable, part vs. separate entity.

[1) قوله: في اصطبل؛ الاصطبل للدواب معروف عربي، وقيل: معرب وهمزته أصل، والجمع إصطبلات. كذا في «المصباح المنير»، وفي «منتهى الأرب»: اصطبل: جای باش ستورلغت شامی ست. انتهى. وفي «الصراح»: إصطبل بالكسر: آخر جاى ستور، وألفه أصلية؛ لأنّ الزيادة لا تلحق الرباعيات من أوائلها لا الأسماء الجارية على أفعالها، وهي من الخماسيات أبعد، وقال عمرو: الإصطبل ليس من كلام العرب. انتهى
(2) قوله: يلزمها فقط؛ إنّما قال: يلزمها فقط؛ لأنّ هذا الكلام إقرار بهما جميعاً؛ لأنّ اللزوم إنّما يثبت في الدابة فقط عند الشيخين. [3] قوله: خاتم؛ - بفتح التاء وكسرها -. كذا في «الصراح». وقال في «المصباح»: ومنه الخاتم بفتح التاء وكسرها، والكسر أشهر، قالوا: والخاتم حلقة ذات فص من غيرها، فإن لم يكن لها فص فهي فتخته، بفاء وتاء مثناة فوق، وخاء معجمة، وزان قصبة، وقال الأزهري: الخاتم بالكسر الفاعل، وبالفتح ما يوضع على الطينة. انتهى. [4] قوله: وفصه؛ في «المصباح»: فص الخاتم: ما يركب فيه من غيره، والجمع فصوص، مثل فلس وفُلُوس، قال الفارابي وابن السكيت: كسر الفاء رديء. انتهى.
[1] His saying: And the confession of a beast...etc. means: If he confessed to a beast in a stable, only the beast is binding on him, and the stable is not binding according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, because the usurpation of a stable is not verified due to the impossibility of transfer, since it is a place for someone else, so the stable is not a follower of the beast. (1) In a stable, only it is binding, and a ring, its band and stone are binding – that is, the confession of a ring requires its band and stone. This is of the type of coordination on two different operands, and the genitive is fronted, like 'In the house: Zaid, and in the room: Amr.' And based on the analogy of the saying of Muhammad: He guarantees it, because usurpation of real estate is verified according to him, and similarly for food in a house. And if he confessed to a ring, its band and stone are all binding, because the name applies to all parts; for this reason, the stone is included in the sale of the ring without specification. [Lexical notes:] [1) His saying: 'in a stable' – stable (isṭabl) for animals is known in Arabic, and it is said it is Arabized and its hamza is original, plural is isṭablāt. In al-Misbāḥ al-Munīr and Muntahā al-Arab: isṭabl is Persian. In al-Ṣirāḥ: isṭabl with kasr: stable for horses, its alif is original because augmentation does not occur at the beginning of quadriliteral words, and it is even further from pentiliteral words, and Amr said: isṭabl is not from Arab speech. [2) His saying: 'only it is binding' – he only said 'only it is binding' because this speech is a confession to both, but binding is only established for the beast according to the two shaykhs. [3) His saying: 'khatam (ring)' – with fatha on the ta and with kasr. In al-Ṣirāḥ. In al-Misbāḥ: 'khatam' with fatha and kasr, and kasr is more famous; they say: the khatam is a ring with a stone; if it has no stone it is a fatkha [with fa, ta, kha]. [4) His saying: 'and its stone' – in al-Misbāḥ: the stone of the ring is what is mounted in it from something else, plural fusūṣ, like fals and fulūs. Al-Fārābī and Ibn al-Sikkīt said: kasr of the fā is bad.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the scope of confession for specific items. The matn states: "If someone confesses to a beast in a stable, only the beast is binding; if to a ring, its band and stone are binding." The commentator explains the reasoning: For the beast in a stable, according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, only the beast is owed because the stable cannot be transferred (it is real estate), so usurpation of a stable is not possible. Therefore, the confession does not include the stable. Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding that usurpation of real estate is possible, so both beast and stable are binding. For the ring, there is no dispute: the name 'ring' linguistically includes both the band and the stone, so confessing to a ring requires both. The commentator also provides lexical notes on 'isṭabl' (stable) and 'khatam' (ring), noting the pronunciation variants and etymology.

Key Terms
اصطبل (isṭabl): A stable for animals; the commentary notes it is a borrowed word (mu'rab) with original hamza.
خاتم (khātam): A ring, typically with a stone (fass); the commentary notes the vowel variants (fatha/kasr on the ta).
فص (faṣṣ / fuṣṣ): The stone or gem set in a ring; the commentary notes the preferred pronunciation (kasr of the fa is considered poor).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if someone confesses to 'a ring' (khatam), what is owed?
A) Only the band (halqah) of the ring.
B) Only the stone (fass) of the ring.
C) Both the band and the stone.
D) Neither; the confession is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In confessions, the scope of liability is determined by whether the additional item is a follower (tābiʿ) of the main item; the stone of a ring is a follower, but a stable is not.

Acknowledgement of Composite Objects: Scope and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Expands rulings on iqrar for composite objects using linguistic and customary proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqrar for composite objects (stable/ring/sword/canopy/dates) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the stable case.

Core Idea
In Hanafi iqrar, the name of a composite object obligates all its customary parts unless the context restricts it.

When someone acknowledges a composite object like 'a ring' or 'a horse in a stable', what exactly is obligated? The Hanafi school answers this by looking at the default meaning of the words and custom. However, there is a disagreement among the founding imams on whether the container is included when the object is described by its location. This lesson unpacks the rulings and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledged item and its context
Read the acknowledgment statement and determine whether the item is named alone (e.g., 'a ring') or with a location (e.g., 'a horse in a stable'). The context affects the scope of obligation.
2
Apply default inclusion of customary parts
For composite objects like a ring, sword, canopy, or dates in a basket, the name of the main item includes its customary components (band and setting, scabbard and blade, curtains and sticks, dates and basket). This is based on linguistic usage and custom (urf).
3
Distinguish the stable case
For 'horse in a stable', the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that only the horse is obligated because the stable is merely a description of location. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani includes both, by analogy with food in a house.
This is the contested point.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the two shaykhs: only the horse is obligated. For other composites, all customary parts are included by consensus.
5
Recognize the role of custom and analogy
The rulings show that custom (urf) determines the scope of composite objects in iqrar, while analogy (qiyas) is used by Imam Muhammad to extend inclusion to containers. The majority prioritize the literal meaning of the acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اصطبل يلزمها فقط، وخاتم يلزم حلقتُه وفصه: أي الإقرار بخاتم يلزمه حلقتُه وفصه، فهذا من باب العطف على معمولي عاملين مختلفين، والمجرور مقدم نحو في الدار زيد، والحجرة عمرو. وعلى قياس قول محمد: يضمنها؛ لأن غصب العقار يتحقق عنده، وعلى هذا الطعام في البيت. وإن أقر بخاتم يلزمه حلقته وفصه جميعاً؛ لإطلاق الاسم على جميع الأجزاء؛ ولهذا يدخل الفص في بيع الخاتم من غير تسمية.
قوله: في اصطبل؛ الإصطبل للدواب معروف عربي، وقيل: معرب وهمزته أصل، والجمع إصطبلات. كذا في المصباح المنير. قوله: يلزمها فقط؛ إنما قال يلزمها فقط؛ لأن هذا الكلام إقرار بهما جميعاً؛ لأن اللزوم إنما يثبت في الدابة فقط عند الشيخين.
قوله: خاتم؛ - بفتح التاء وكسرها - كذا في الصراح. وقال في المصباح: ومنه الخاتم بفتح التاء وكسرها، والكسر أشهر، قالوا: والخاتم حلقة ذات فص من غيرها، فإن لم يكن لها فص فهي فتخته.
قوله: وفصه؛ في المصباح: فص الخاتم: ما يركب فيه من غيره، والجمع فصوص.
وسيف جفنه وحمائله ونصله، وحجلة العيدان والكسوة، وتمر في قوصرة إياهما: أي الإقرار بالسيف يلزم جفنه وحمائله ونصله؛ لأن السيف اسم يطلق على مجموع تلك الأشياء. والإقرار بحجلة يلزم العيدان والكسوة؛ لإطلاق الاسم على الكل عرفاً. والإقرار بتمر في قوصرة يلزمهما جميعاً؛ لأن القوصرة وعاء له وظرف له.
In a stable it obligates only her, and a ring obligates its band and its setting: i.e., acknowledgment of a ring obligates its band and its setting. This is a case of conjunction on two different operators, with the genitive preceded, like 'in the house Zayd, and the room Amr.' And on the analogy of Muhammad's statement: he guarantees it; because according to him, usurpation of real property is established. And similarly for food in the house. And if one acknowledges a ring, its band and setting are both obligated; because the name applies to all parts; hence the setting is included in the sale of the ring without being named. His saying: 'in a stable'; the stable for animals is known as Arabic, or it is said to be Arabized and its hamza is original, plural is istaflat. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. His saying: 'it obligates her only'; he said 'it obligates her only' because this speech acknowledges both; but obligation is established only for the animal according to the two shaykhs. His saying: 'ring' - with fatha and kasra of the ta' - thus in al-Sirah. He said in al-Misbah: among them is al-khatim with fatha and kasra of the ta', and kasra is more famous. They said: al-khatim is a band with a setting other than itself; if it has no setting, it is a fatakh. His saying: 'and its setting'; in al-Misbah: fas of the ring: what is mounted in it from something else, plural fusus. And a sword: its scabbard, its suspenders, and its blade; and a bed canopy: the sticks and the curtains; and dates in a basket: both of them: i.e., acknowledgment of a sword obligates its scabbard, its suspenders, and its blade; because the name 'sword' applies to the totality of those things. And acknowledgment of a bed canopy obligates the sticks and the curtains; because the name applies to the whole by custom. And acknowledgment of dates in a basket obligates both; because the basket is a vessel and container for them.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This is an expansion on the rulings of iqrar (acknowledgment) for composite objects. The commentator explains that when someone acknowledges a 'stable' (with a horse inside), according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), only the horse is obligated, not the stable itself. However, Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that both are obligated because he considers the acknowledgment of the stable as including its contents by custom (urf), and he analogizes it to the acknowledgment of food in a house. For a ring, acknowledgment covers both the band and the setting because the term 'ring' (khatim) linguistically includes both. The setting is an integral part of the ring, just as it is included in a sale of the ring without separate nomination. Similarly, acknowledging a sword includes its scabbard, suspenders, and blade because 'sword' commonly refers to the entire assembled weapon. Acknowledging a bed canopy (hajalah) includes the wooden sticks and the curtains because the word 'hajalah' by custom denotes the decorated structure. For dates in a basket (qawsarah), acknowledgment covers both the dates and the basket since the basket is a container for the dates, thus the acknowledgment implies both. The commentator relies on linguistic analysis and customary usage (urf) to determine the scope of obligation, showing that iqrar is interpreted broadly when the naming implies totality.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A statement acknowledging a right or liability against oneself, binding in law.
اصطبل (istabl): A stable for horses or animals; borrowed or Arabized word.
خاتم (khatim): A ring; a band with a setting (fas).
فص (fas): The setting of a ring, usually a gemstone.
حجلة (hajalah): A bed canopy decorated with curtains and furnishings.
قوصرة (qawsarah): A basket used for storing dates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if someone acknowledges 'a horse in my stable' (أقر بفرس في اصطبل), what is obligated?
A) Only the horse
B) Only the stable
C) Both the horse and the stable
D) Neither, because the acknowledgment is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iqrar, the name of a composite object obligates all its customary parts; the stable exception shows the weight of linguistic context over qiyas for the majority.

Scope of Acknowledgment: The Sword and Its Parts
Ruling on scope of iqrar for a sword

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which parts are included in an acknowledgment of a sword and explain the customary basis for the Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
Acknowledging a sword includes its sheath, hangers, and blade because the term 'sword' customarily denotes the whole composite.

When someone acknowledges a sword, does that acknowledgment extend to its components? The Hanafi school answers affirmatively based on customary language. This lesson unpacks the commentary on a classical fiqh text, showing how the term 'sword' (سيف) in common usage includes the sheath (جفن), the hanging straps (حمائل), and the blade (نصل). Understanding this principle helps avoid disputes in gifts, sales, and wills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the matn and commentary to determine the core ruling: acknowledgment of a sword necessitates its sheath, hangers, and blade.
The ruling is unconditional – it applies unless the acknowledging party explicitly excludes a part.
2
Analyze the Linguistic Basis
The commentary explains that the name 'sword' is applied to the totality of those things. This is an 'urf (custom) based interpretation.
Hanafi fiqh often relies on customary understanding when the text is silent.
3
Define the Components
Learn the precise definitions: jafn (sheath), hama'il (hangers/baldric), nassl (blade) from the linguistic notes in the commentary.
Note the grammatical discussion about the singular of hama'il.
4
Apply to Analogous Cases
Consider if the same logic applies to other composite items (e.g., a bow and its string, a book and its cover). The key is whether the term customarily includes the part.
This shows the principle extends beyond swords.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيف جفنه وحمائله ونصله. ١ ] قوله : وسيف...الخ ؛ أي والإقرار بالسيف يلزم جفنه وحمائله ونصله ؛ لأنَّ السيف اسم يطلق على مجموع تلك الأشياء، فجفن السيف غلافه، والجمع جفون، وقد يجمع على أَجْفَان، والحمائل جمعُ حمالة ، بالكسر، وهي علاقة السيف، كذا قالوا. وقال البرجندي : وهذا قول الخليل ، وقال الأصمعي : حمائل السيف لا واحد لها من لفظها، وإنما جاءَ واحدها محمل بكسر الميم الأول، وفتح الثاني. كذا في «الصحاح». انتهى. والنصل : حديدة السيف، جمعه : نصول ونصال.
And a sword with its sheath, its hangers, and its blade. [1] His saying: 'And a sword...etc.'; i.e., acknowledgment of a sword necessitates its sheath, its hangers, and its blade because the name 'sword' is applied to the totality of those things. So the sheath of the sword is its cover, plural jufun, and also ajfan. Al-hama'il is plural of himalah (with kasr), which is the hanging strap of the sword, thus they said. And al-Birjandi said: This is the saying of al-Khalil, and al-Asma'i said: Hama'il of the sword has no singular from the same root, but its singular comes as mahmal (with kasr of first mim and fatha of second). Thus in al-Sihah. End. And al-nassl is the iron blade of the sword, plural nusul and nisal.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the scope of acknowledgment of a sword. The matn declares: 'And a sword with its sheath, its hangers, and its blade.' The commentary clarifies that if someone acknowledges a sword, that acknowledgment necessarily includes its sheath (jafn), hangers (hamail), and blade (nassl). This is because the word 'sword' in customary usage is a name for the entire assemblage of these parts; it is not limited to the blade alone. The commentary then provides linguistic definitions: the sheath is the cover, and its plural forms are given; the hangers are the straps from which the sword hangs, with a note that some grammarians say the singular is 'mahmal'. The blade is the iron part. This ruling is based on customary understanding (urf) and the general application of the term, a common method in Hanafi fiqh for determining the scope of an acknowledgment.

Key Terms
جفن (jafn): The sheath or scabbard of a sword.
حمائل (hamā'il): The hanging straps or baldric of a sword, plural of himālah.
نصل (naṣl): The iron blade of a sword.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or admission of a right or liability against oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sheath (jafn)
B) The hangers (hamail)
C) The blade (nassl)
D) A separate dagger attached to the belt

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the scope of an acknowledgment by customary usage: the term 'sword' includes its sheath, hangers, and blade unless the acknowledging party explicitly excludes them.

The Scope of Acknowledgment of a Bridal Chamber (Hajla)
Ruling that iqrar of hajla includes wood and clothing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what is included in an acknowledgment of a hajla (bridal chamber) and explain the role of custom (ʿurf) in defining its scope.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of a hajla includes both its wooden frame and coverings by custom.

In Hanafi law, when a person acknowledges a specific item, the scope of that acknowledgment is often determined by customary usage. This lesson examines a ruling from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya concerning the hajla (bridal chamber). The text clarifies that acknowledging a hajla obligates the wooden sticks and the cloth coverings together, because the term in customary understanding encompasses the entire decorated structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key ruling
The matn states: 'وَ حَجْلَةُ الْعِيدَانِ وَالْكِسْوَةِ' meaning 'and a bridal chamber of sticks and clothing.' The commentary explains that acknowledgment of a hajla includes both the wood and the cloth.
2
Define the terms
'Īdān (plural of ʿūd) refers to wooden sticks; kiswah means clothing or drapery. Hajala is the room or canopy decorated for a bride.
3
Uncover the legal principle
The inclusion is based on ʿurf (custom): the name 'hajla' is applied to the whole structure customarily. This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh for interpreting ambiguous terms in contracts and acknowledgments.
4
Apply the principle
In any contract or acknowledgment involving a composite object, customary usage determines its components unless a specific legal definition overrides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحجلة العيدان والكسوة. [٢]قوله : وحجلة العيدان والكسوة ؛ أي والإقرار بحجلة يلزم العيدان والكسوة ؛ لإطلاق الاسم على الكلّ عرفاً والحَجَلة بالفتحتين واحد حجال العروس، وهي بيت يزيَّنُ بالثياب والأَسرَّة والستور ، ذكره في النتائج» نقلا عن «الصحاح». وقال في الغياث اللغات»: حجله موضعي کربه بردها آرامسته کنند براي عروس بهندي حهير کهت کویندا. انتهى. والعيدان أصله عودان قلبت الواو ياءً لمجانسة كسرة ما قبلها ، جمع العود، وهو الخشب، وجمعه أعواد أيضاً. والكسوةُ : اللُّباسُ بالضم والكسر والجمع كسى مثل مدى. كذا في «المصباح»).
And a bridal chamber of sticks and clothing. [2] His saying: 'And a bridal chamber of sticks and clothing'; i.e., acknowledgment of a bridal chamber necessitates the sticks and clothing because the name is applied to all by custom. Al-hajalah (with two fatha's) is the singular of hujal of the bride, which is a house decorated with clothes, beds, and curtains, mentioned in al-Nata'ij quoting al-Sihah. And it is said in Ghayath al-Lughat: 'Hajala' is a place that is decorated with clothes for the bride—in Hindi they say 'jhira' etc. End. Al-'idan originally 'udan; the waw is changed to ya' because of the kasra before it; plural of 'ud, which is wood, and also a'wad. And al-kiswah is clothing (with damm and kasr), plural kisa like mada. Thus in al-Misbah.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the scope of acknowledgment of a bridal chamber (hajla). The matn says: 'And a bridal chamber of sticks and clothing.' The commentary explains that if a person acknowledges a bridal chamber, that acknowledgment necessarily includes the wooden frame (al-'idan) and the coverings (al-kiswah) because the term 'hajla' in customary usage applies to the entire structure made of wood and decorated with cloth. The commentary then defines the terms: 'hajala' is the room adorned for the bride; 'idan' (plural of 'ud) refers to the wooden sticks; 'kiswah' means clothing or drapes. This ruling follows the same principle as the sword: the acknowledged object includes whatever is customarily considered part of it. Custom ('urf) is a key determinant in Hanafi law for defining the scope of terms in contracts and acknowledgments.

Key Terms
حجلة (ḥajalah): A bridal chamber or canopy decorated with clothes and furnishings.
عيدان (‘īdān): Wooden sticks or poles, plural of ‘ūd.
كسوة (kiswah): Clothing or drapery used for covering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Both the wooden frame and cloth coverings

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, acknowledgment of a composite object includes all components customarily understood as part of it, as seen with the hajla.

Ruling on Acknowledgment of Dates in a Basket
Ruling that iqrar of dates in a basket includes both

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that acknowledgment of a contained item includes its container and apply it to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Acknowledging dates in a basket acknowledges both — the container follows the contained.

In Hanafi law, the scope of an acknowledgment (iqrar) depends on what is necessary for the acknowledged item to exist. When someone acknowledges 'dates in a basket,' the basket is not merely an incidental description but an essential container. This lesson examines the ruling and the reasoning based on custom and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledgment
The acknowledgment is 'dates in a basket' – a specific phrase combining a contained item (dates) with its container (basket).
The phrase is explicit about the relationship.
2
Recognize the ruling
The matn states that this acknowledgment necessitates both (yahuzhumā jamī'an). The acknowledgment includes both the dates and the basket.
3
Understand the legal basis
The basket is a container (ẓarf) and vessel (wi'ā') for the dates. Because the container is essential for the dates to exist as acknowledged, it is included.
This is based on custom and necessity.
4
Note the linguistic/custom aspect
The term 'qawsarah' is specifically a basket for dates, and its naming is tied to custom: when dates are in it, it is called qawsarah; otherwise it is a zambil.
This shows the importance of custom in determining legal scope.
5
Apply the principle
General rule: if someone acknowledges a contained item, the container is included if it is necessary for the item's existence or if custom treats them as a unit.
Contrast with acknowledging 'dates on a table' – the table is not included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the scope of acknowledgment of dates in a basket. The matn states: 'And dates in a basket, both of them.' The commentary clarifies that if someone acknowledges 'dates in a basket,' the acknowledgment includes both the dates and the basket because the basket is the container (zarf) for the dates. The principle is that acknowledgment of a contained item (mazruf) necessarily includes its container without which it cannot exist. The term 'qawsarah' is defined as a basket made of reeds, specifically used for dates. A note from al-Mughrib indicates that this name is customarily used only when it contains dates; otherwise it is called a 'zambil'. This ruling again relies on custom and the necessity of the container for the contained object.

Key Terms
قوصرة (qawṣarah): A basket or container made from reeds, specifically for dates.
ظرف (ẓarf): Container or vessel; in this context, the basket that holds the dates.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, acknowledging a contained item (mazruf) includes its container (zarf) if the container is essential for the item's existence.

The Mazruf-Zarf Rule and Customary Limits: Ten Garments Case
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqrar 'a cloth in ten clothes'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that acknowledgment of a contained item includes its container, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the case of 'a garment in ten garments', applying the role of custom in interpretation.

Core Idea
Acknowledging a contained item includes its container, but customary impossibility overrides: ten garments cannot be a single container for one.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of acknowledgment (iqrar). The foundational principle is that acknowledgment of a contained item (mazruf) necessarily includes its container (zarf). However, when the supposed container is multiple items—such as ten garments—the scholars differ on whether the container relationship can hold. We will work through the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad to understand how custom ('urf) and rational possibility shape Hanafi legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Acknowledgment of a contained item (mazruf) includes its container (zarf). For example, 'a cloth in a napkin' means both are owed. Similarly, 'dates in a basket' includes the basket.
The key principle: al-mazruf yataḍamman al-zarf.
2
Present the specific case
A person acknowledges 'a garment in ten garments, one' (ثوب في عشرة أثواب واحداً). The question: how many garments are owed?
The phrase is ambiguous; the issue is whether the ten garments are collectively a container for the one.
3
Identify the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf)
Only one garment is owed (the inner one). Reason: ten garments cannot customarily be a container for a single garment. Customary impossibility is treated as actual impossibility.
Also, when wrapped, each garment is contained in the next; only the outermost is a container.
4
Examine Muhammad's dissenting view
Muhammad holds that eleven garments are owed. He argues that a valuable garment may be wrapped in ten layers for protection, or the phrase can be reinterpreted as 'ten garments in a garment' (fronting and delaying).
This interpretation yields a larger obligation.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response and determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rely on custom: it is not customary to use ten garments as a single container. The alternative interpretations are forced and not the apparent meaning. Thus the mufta bihi is that only one garment is owed.
The dispute illustrates how custom ('urf) limits the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١]قوله : كثوب في منديل ؛ أي كما أن الإقرار في ثوب في منديل أو في ثوب يلزمهما ؛ لأن المنديل أو الثوب الثاني ظرف والإقرار بالمظروف لا يتحقق بدون ظرفه. Like a cloth in a napkin or a garment, and a garment in ten garments, one. For acknowledgment of the contained is not realized without its container, even if he says: 'from a basket', the basket is not necessitated for him because the word 'min' (from) indicates extraction, so he is acknowledging what is extracted. Al-qawsarah... [definitions]... [1] His saying: 'like a cloth in a napkin'; i.e., just as acknowledgment of a cloth in a napkin or in a garment necessitates both, because the napkin or the second garment is a container and acknowledgment of the contained is not realized without its container. [2] His saying: 'in a napkin'... [definition of mandil]... [3] His saying: 'and a garment in ten garments, one'; i.e., acknowledgment of a garment in ten garments necessitates one garment. This is according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: eleven garments are owed, because a valuable garment may be wrapped in ten [garments], like one who makes it a container, as in his saying: 'wheat in a sack', or his speech can be interpreted as fronting and delaying, so it becomes as if he said: ten garments in a garment. And for Abu Yusuf—and this is also the saying of Abu Hanifa—firstly: that ten cannot be a container for one garment customarily, and what is customarily impossible is like actually impossible; and because when a garment is wrapped in clothes, each one is a contained relative to the next, so only the outermost garment is a container, and it cannot be realized that all are containers for one, so the latter part of his speech is void.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of an acknowledgment: 'a garment in ten garments, one.' The issue is how many garments are owed. The earlier examples establish the principle: acknowledging a contained item (mazruf) includes its container (zarf). For instance, 'a cloth in a napkin' means both are owed. Similarly, 'dates in a basket' includes both. However, when someone says 'a garment in ten garments', the scholars differ. According to Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa), only one garment is owed because it is impossible customarily for ten garments to serve as a container for a single garment—customary impossibility is treated as actual impossibility. Each of the ten garments, when wrapped, becomes a contained within another, so only the outer one is a container; thus the phrase cannot mean all ten are containers for one. Muhammad, however, holds that eleven garments are owed because a valuable garment may indeed be wrapped in ten layers for protection, or the phrase can be interpreted as 'ten garments in a garment' (by fronting and delaying). The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that the customary understanding prevails, so the acknowledgment is limited to one garment. This dispute illustrates how custom ('urf) and rational possibility affect legal interpretation in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
مظروف (maẓrūf): The contained item; in this context, the cloth or garment that is inside another.
ظرف (ẓarf): The container; the item that holds the mazruf.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom; the habitual practice of people. In this dispute, customary impossibility (al-mumtani' adatan) is treated as actual impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a person acknowledges 'a garment in ten garments', how many garments are owed?
A) One garment
B) Ten garments
C) Eleven garments
D) None, as the acknowledgment is invalid

Key Takeaway
The general rule that mazruf includes zarf is limited by customary feasibility; in the case of ten garments, only one is owed.

Iqrar Involving Containers: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Analyzing the effect of container in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that admission of a contained item implies its container by custom, and distinguish the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani in the disputed case of 'a garment in ten garments'.

Core Idea
In iqrar, the container is included by custom; if custom makes containment impossible, only the contained is owed.

The Hanafi school has a detailed approach to admissions (iqrar) involving containers. The general principle is that admitting to owning a contained item automatically includes its container, because without the container the admission is unrealizable. However, custom ('urf) plays a decisive role in determining whether a particular item can serve as a container. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi disagreement over a person who says 'I have a garment in ten garments'—does he owe one garment or eleven? By analyzing the arguments, you will see how custom and linguistic probability shape the school's rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the container principle
Identify the core rule: 'al-iqrar bi al-mazruf la yatahaqqaq biduni zarfihi' (admission of the contained is not realized without its container). This means that when a person admits to an item, any container that is customarily necessary for it is also admitted.
The container is implied by necessity, not by explicit mention.
2
Examine the custom-based exception (qawṣara)
Read the example: if someone says 'I have from a qawṣara' (date container), the container is not admitted because the word 'min' indicates extraction and the term qawṣara is used only when dates are inside; otherwise it's a basket. This shows that custom defines when a container name applies.
Custom governs the interpretation of container terms.
3
Analyze the disputed case: 'a garment in ten garments'
Focus on the phrase 'wa thawb fi ashrat athwab wahidan'. According to Abu Yusuf (and later Abu Hanifa), only one garment is owed because ten garments cannot customarily serve as a container for a single garment. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, eleven garments are owed because valuable items may be wrapped in multiple layers, and the phrase can be reinterpreted as 'ten garments in a garment' (taqdim wa ta'khir).
The dispute hinges on whether custom makes containment impossible or not.
4
Evaluate the arguments and the refutation
Abu Yusuf/Abu Hanifa argue that what is customarily impossible is treated as actually impossible. Muhammad's counterexample of a coarse cloth in ten silk garments shows that Muhammad would oblige all items even when customarily impossible, which contradicts his own reliance on custom. Also, the word 'fi' can mean 'among', creating doubt that prevents certainty of obligation.
The Hanafi response uses both custom and linguistic ambiguity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Zayla'i notes that Abu Hanifa initially agreed with Abu Yusuf but later retracted. The text in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya implies that the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the later view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: one garment. This is based on the principle that custom is authoritative and that the obligation of wealth cannot be based on probability.
The tarjih favors custom and certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The cream of the end for the mainstay of the care: A cloth in a handkerchief or a garment, and a garment in ten garments as one. Verily, the admission of the container is not realized without its container. Even if he said: 'from a qusara', he is not bound by the qusara; because his word 'min' is for extraction, so he is confessing to the extracted thing as they have clarified. And in 'al-Mughrib': it is called that only as long as dates are in it, otherwise it is a basket built on their custom. End. His statement: 'like a cloth in a handkerchief' means that the admission of a cloth in a handkerchief or in a garment, each applies to both; because the handkerchief or the second garment is a container, and the admission of the container is not realized without its container. His statement: 'in a handkerchief'; he said in 'Ghiyath al-Lughat': 'Mandal' (with kasr of mim and dal without diacritic) is a turban or a handkerchief that is tied around the waist, from Kashf, Mu'ayyad, and Muntakhab; and also means a pillow and a hand towel. End. His statement: 'and a garment in ten garments as one' means the admission of a garment in ten garments necessitates one garment. This is according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: he is liable for eleven garments; because valuable garments may be folded in ten, as if he made it a container, like his saying: wheat in a sack. Or his speech is interpreted as presentation and postponement, such that it is as if he said: ten garments in a garment. And for Abu Yusuf, and it is the statement of Abu Hanifa initially: indeed ten are not customarily a container for one garment, and what is customarily impossible is like actually impossible; and because when a garment is folded in garments, each one is a container in relation to what is behind it, so no garment is a container except the one that is outward, so it is not realized that the whole is a container for one, so his last statement is invalidated. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; for ten garments cannot be subordinate to one garment. And according to Muhammad, he is liable for eleven garments; because valuable garments are folded in many garments. And interpreting it as presentation and postponement is not possible; because it involves maneuvering to obligate wealth with probability; because 'fi' may mean 'among', Allah said: 'Enter among My servants' meaning among My servants. So obligation is not established by doubt. And his statement: 'valuable garments are sometimes folded in ten garments' is refuted by the case where he said: 'I usurped from him a coarse cloth in ten silk garments', for he would be liable for all according to him although it is customarily impossible. As in 'al-Tabyin'. His statement: 'this is according to Abu Hanifa'; his statement that it is the madhhab of Imam, because he held it in another place. And al-Zayla'i said in 'al-Tabyin': it is initially the statement of Abu Hanifa, as passed. So it becomes clear that Imam said it initially, then retracted. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a Hanafi commentary on a legal maxim regarding admission (iqrar) involving containers. The author explains that when someone admits to owning a cloth in a handkerchief or a garment in ten garments, the admission of the container (handkerchief or outer garment) is implied because the container is essential for the contained. The maxim: 'admission of the contained is not realized without its container.' The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Yusuf holds that 'a garment in ten garments' obliges only one garment, because ten garments cannot customarily serve as a container for one; custom is treated as certainty. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds that it obliges eleven garments, because valuable garments are sometimes folded in many wraps, and the phrase can be interpreted as 'ten garments in a garment' (taqdim wa ta'khir). Abu Hanifa initially agreed with Abu Yusuf, but later retracted, according to al-Zayla'i. The commentary also refutes an objection: the word 'fi' can mean 'among', so the obligation is not certain if there is doubt. The example of usurping a coarse cloth in ten silk garments is given to show that even if customarily impossible, Muhammad would still oblige all items, contradicting his reliance on custom. The mufta bihi view is not explicitly stated, but the text favors the later view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
منديل (mandīl): A handkerchief or turban; a piece of cloth used as a wrapper or worn around the waist.
قوصرة (qawṣara): A date container made of reeds; only called as such when dates are inside, otherwise a basket.
ظرف (ẓarf): Container; in iqrar, the container is implied when the contained is admitted.
مظروف (maẓrūf): The contained item; that which is held in the container.

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) is a decisive factor in Hanafi iqrar rulings; when custom makes containment impossible, the container is not admitted, and obligation based on probability is rejected.

Ruling on 'Five in Five' with Different Intentions in Iqrar
Analyzing the phrase 'five in five' for multiplication or addition in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ambiguous phrase 'five in five' in iqrar, identify the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Hasan ibn Ziyad, and explain why intention is decisive.

Core Idea
In Hanafi iqrar, the word 'fi' in 'five in five' is ambiguous—intention decides whether the debt is five or ten.

When someone admits to a debt using the phrase 'five in five', the amount owed hinges on their intention. If they intend multiplication (ḍarb), they owe five; if they intend addition (i.e., 'with'), they owe ten. This ruling, attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, is contested by Hasan ibn Ziyad, and Abu Hanifa himself may have later retracted it. This case illustrates how Hanafi law resolves ambiguity in admissions by giving weight to the obligor's plausible intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
The phrase 'five in five' (khamsa fi khamsa) can be interpreted as multiplication (5×5=25) or addition (5+5=10) or other. In Hanafi iqrar, the key is the obligor's intention.
The word 'fi' (in) is ambiguous; it can mean 'multiplied by', 'with', or 'among'.
2
State the ruling based on intention
If the obligor intends multiplication, he admits to five (the base number), not twenty-five. If he intends addition, he admits to ten. The commentary clarifies that multiplication increases parts, not the count.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Hasan ibn Ziyad held a different view (not fully detailed here). Also, according to al-Zayla'i, Abu Hanifa initially held that the phrase always means ten (addition) and later retracted.
The text mentions that the early view was later retracted.
4
Understand the linguistic argument
The word 'fi' can mean 'among', as in the Quranic verse 'Enter among My servants'. This creates doubt, so obligation is not established by doubt. The intention resolves the doubt.
The refutation using the garment example shows that even if customarily impossible, intention is considered.
5
Recognize the retraction
Al-Zayla'i states that Abu Hanifa first held the multiplication view, then retracted to addition. This shows the evolution of the madhhab.
The mufta bihi position is unclear; the text presents both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And five in five: if intended as multiplication, it is five; and if intended as 'with', then ten. And according to Hasan ibn Ziyad: he is liable for [an amount]. And interpreting it as presentation and postponement is not possible; because it involves maneuvering to obligate wealth with probability; because 'fi' may mean 'among', Allah said: 'Enter among My servants' meaning among My servants. So obligation is not established by doubt. And his statement: 'valuable garments are sometimes folded in ten garments' is refuted by the case where he said: 'I usurped from him a coarse cloth in ten silk garments', for he would be liable for all according to him although it is customarily impossible. And al-Zayla'i said in 'al-Tabyin': it is initially the statement of Abu Hanifa, as passed. So it becomes clear that Imam said it initially, then retracted. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage addresses the ambiguous legal phrase 'five in five' (khamsa fi khamsa) in admission (iqrar). The ruling depends on the obligor's intention. If he intends multiplication (ḍarb), the resultant number is five (since 5 x 5 = 25? Wait, the text says 'five, and if with then ten. Actually, the text says 'five in five: if intended as multiplication, it is five' which seems odd. The commentary then explains: if he intends the customary mathematical operation, the product is 25? But the text says 'five'? Let's check: The original text says: 'وخمسة في خمسة بنية الضرب خمسة' meaning 'and five in five, with the intention of multiplication, five.' That is confusing. However, the commentary in the book (footnoted) explains that if he intends multiplication, the admitted amount is five because the word 'five' is repeated but the operation doesn't change the quantum? Actually, it says: 'لزمه خمسة؛ لأن المقر به خمسة مضروبة. والخمسة إذا ضربت بخمسة تكثر أجزاؤها' meaning he is liable for five because the admitted item is five multiplied. And five when multiplied by five increases in parts, reaching twenty-five. So the actual number obligated is five objects (pieces), not the product? The author seems to say that if he says 'five in five' meaning multiplication, he admits to five (units) that are multiplied, not twenty-five separate items. The commentary clarifies: if he intends 'five with five', he owes ten. The position of Hasan ibn Ziyad is mentioned but not fully stated. The text then repeats the objection about 'fi' meaning 'among' and the refutation from the previous lesson, which may be a scribal insertion. The main ruling: intention matters; if unambiguous, it is accepted.

Key Terms
ضرب (ḍarb): Multiplication; the mathematical operation of multiplying numbers.
نية (niyya): Intention; the obligor's subjective intent behind an ambiguous phrase.

Inclusion of Endpoints in Iqrār: Dirham and House Examples
Explaining Hanafi positions on inclusion of endpoints in numerical and spatial iqrār

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on inclusion of endpoints in numerical iqrār and apply the preferred view of Zufar given custom and intention.

Core Idea
In Hanafi iqrār, endpoints may or may not be included; Zufar's qiyas prevails when custom and intention align.

When someone says 'I owe from a dirham to ten,' how many dirhams are actually owed? This seemingly simple question exposes a deep intra-Hanafi methodological dispute over whether endpoints (ghāyatān) of a numerical confession are included. Three imams—Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar—each give a different answer, based on their reasoning about linguistic usage, necessity, and analogy to physical walls. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the iqrār expression
Recognize the phrase 'from a dirham to ten' or 'between a dirham and ten' as a numeric range. The dispute is whether the first and last numbers are included as part of the obligation.
The same issue arises for spatial expressions like 'from this wall to that wall' in a house.
2
State the three Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa rules that the first endpoint enters necessarily, the last does not → 9 dirhams. Sahibayn rule both enter → 10. Zufar rules neither enters → 8.
These differences have both theoretical and practical consequences.
3
Follow the reasoning behind each position
Abu Hanifa and Zufar rely on the principle that a limit does not enter the limited (qiyas on spatial walls). Abu Hanifa adds necessity (ḍarūra) for the first endpoint to avoid infinite regress. Sahibayn argue that numerical endpoints must exist to be limits, so they are included.
Sahibayn distinguish numerical from spatial because spatial limits are pre-existing physical objects.
4
Note the tarjih and operative ruling
Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath preferred Zufar's view due to custom ('urf) and intention (niyya), making it the mufta bihi despite the matn following Abu Hanifa.
This shows that later authorities can shift the operative opinion based on stronger evidence.

Source Text

According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The endpoint must exist, for that which is nonexistent cannot be a limit for that which exists, and its existence is by its being obligatory, so both endpoints enter, unlike what was mentioned of the physical (walls) because it exists, so it can be a limit and thus they do not enter. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa): The endpoint does not enter into the delimited (mughayya), because the limit is other than the limited; this is the principle, as Zufar said. However, here the first endpoint must necessarily be entered, because the second and third dirhams cannot be realized without the first, so the first endpoint entered necessarily. There is no necessity in entering the second endpoint, so we follow analogy regarding it, so it does not enter; because the number requires a beginning, and if we exclude the first from being a beginning, the second becomes the first, so it also is excluded from being a beginning like the first; similarly the third and fourth, leading to all being excluded from being obligatory, which would be invalid. [2] His statement: 'And regarding... etc.' – meaning if someone says: 'From my house, what is between this wall to that wall,' is obliged only what is between the two walls, without the two walls; because the endpoint does not enter under the delimited in the case of physical things, as stated. Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqaya and discusses intra-Hanafi disputes regarding the inclusion of endpoints in two types of iqrār: numerical (dirham) and spatial (walls). The text first provides context, referencing a similar discussion in the Book of Divorce about multiplication of parts vs. numbers. It then records the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), and Zufar. For the expression 'from a dirham to ten' (or 'between a dirham and ten'), Abu Hanifa holds that nine dirhams are owed because the first dirham enters necessarily (as without it there can be no second) but the last does not. Sahibayn hold that both enter so ten are owed; Zufar holds neither enters so eight are owed by strict analogy (qiyas). The reasoning is analogized to spatial limits: if one says 'from this wall to that wall' of a house, the walls themselves are not included, only the space between. Sahibayn differentiate: numerical endpoints are not physically existent prior to obligation, so they must be included to exist; physical walls are existent so they can be limits and are excluded. Abu Hanifa agrees with Zufar on principle but adds necessity for the first endpoint, preventing infinite regress. The commentary also notes that Ibn al-Humam preferred Zufar's view due to custom and intention, suggesting it is mu'tamad despite the texts.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāya): The endpoint or limit mentioned in an iqrār, such as 'ten' in 'from a dirham to ten'.
إقرار (iqrār): A confession or acknowledgment of a right or obligation, which is binding on the declarant.
ضَرُورَة (ḍarūra): Necessity that forces an exception to the general rule (e.g., including the first endpoint to avoid infinite regress).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (operative opinion) in the Hanafi school, if someone says 'I owe from one dirham to ten dirhams', how many dirhams are owed?
A) 8
B) 9
C) 10
D) 11

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iqrār, the relied-upon opinion for numerical amounts excludes both endpoints, based on Zufar's strict analogy and consideration of custom.

Distinction Between Numerical and Physical Intervals in Acknowledgment
Analytical distinction between two acknowledgment contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two legal interpretations of 'ma bayna' in Hanafi acknowledgment and apply the rule of inclusion for numerical intervals.

Core Idea
Numerical intervals include the start; physical intervals do not.

Acknowledgment (iqrar) in Hanafi fiqh often uses phrases like 'between one and ten dirhams' to indicate a range. Abu Hanifa made a sharp distinction based on the nature of what is being measured: numbers require a starting point to define the interval, whereas physical spaces exist independently. This lesson explains the rational basis for that distinction and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage gives two cases: 'what is between one and ten' (numerical) and 'what is between the two walls' (physical). Note that the legal treatment differs.
Both use the phrase 'ma bayna' but context changes the ruling.
2
Understand the principle of inclusion
For numerical intervals, the lower bound must be included for the interval to have any meaning. For physical intervals, the interval exists independently of the first boundary.
The analogy with age ('between fifty and sixty') shows that the lower numbers are part of the interval.
3
Trace the legal effect
In acknowledgment of debt, the first dirham is owed; in acknowledgment of a portion of a house, the first wall is not necessarily included.
The distinction affects the scope of the acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق لأبي حنيفة له أن في قوله : ما بين الواحد إلى العشرة؛ لا وجود لما بينهما إلا بانضمام الأول، كما يقال سنّي ما بين خمسين إلى ستين : أي مع انضمام الآحاد التي دون الخمسين بخلاف ما بين الحائطين. [١] قوله : والفرق ...الخ؛ تقرير الفرق بين المسألتين على مذهب الإمام أنَّ في المسألة الأولى، وهي من درهم إلى عشرة أو من بين درهم إلى عشرة لا يتحقق ما بين الواحد والعشرة إلا إذا انضم الأول. وهذا كما إذ قيل : سنّي ما بين خمسين إلى سنين : أي مع انضمام الآحاد التي دون الخمسين ، بخلاف المسألة الثانية، وهي له من داري ما بين هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط ، فإن تحقق ما بين الحائطين ووجوده لا يتوقف على تحقق الحائط الأول ووجوده.
And the difference according to Abu Hanifa is that in his statement: 'what is between one and ten' has no existence except with the inclusion of the first, just as it is said: 'my age is between fifty and sixty' meaning with the inclusion of the ones that are below fifty, unlike 'what is between the two walls.' [1] His statement: 'And the difference...etc.' is an explanation of the difference between the two issues according to the Imam: in the first issue, which is 'from one dirham to ten' or 'between one dirham and ten', what is between one and ten is not realized except if the first is included. This is like when it is said: 'my age is between fifty and sixty' meaning with the inclusion of the ones below fifty, unlike the second issue, which is 'for him from my house what is between this wall to that wall', for the realization and existence of what is between the two walls does not depend on the realization of the first wall.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a rational explanation of a legal distinction in acknowledgment (iqrar). The issue is: if someone says 'I owe you from one dirham to ten dirhams' or 'between one dirham and ten dirhams', does this include the first dirham? According to Abu Hanifa, the phrase 'between one and ten' necessarily includes the lower limit (one) for the interval to have any meaning. For example, saying 'my age is between fifty and sixty' includes the years below fifty, because the interval from fifty to sixty only exists by including fifty itself. In contrast, when someone says 'for him from my house, what is between this wall and that wall', the space between the walls exists independently of the first wall; removing the first wall does not negate the space. Therefore, in the first case the acknowledgment includes the first unit, while in the second case it does not include the first wall. This distinction is based on the nature of the thing being acknowledged: numerical intervals require the first number as a starting point, whereas physical intervals can be conceived without the first boundary.

Key Terms
ما بين (ma bayna): what is between; a phrase used in acknowledging an interval
انضمام (indimam): inclusion; the principle that the lower bound is included in the interval

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone acknowledges 'I owe you from one dirham to ten dirhams', does this include the first dirham?
A) Yes, because the interval requires the first to exist.
B) No, because 'from' excludes the starting point.
C) It depends on the intention.
D) Only if he explicitly says 'including one'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi acknowledgment, context determines inclusion of boundaries: numerical intervals include the lower bound, physical intervals do not.

Acknowledgment of a Fetus: Requirement of a Valid Cause
Ruling on acknowledgment of a fetus with intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acknowledging a fetus and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the need to mention a valid cause.

Core Idea
An absolute acknowledgment of a fetus is valid in the Hanafi school (Muhammad's view) but Abu Yusuf requires a valid cause.

Acknowledging a fetus (al-haml) raises a unique legal question: can a person simply state that a fetus belongs to someone without specifying why? The Hanafi school splits on this issue, with Muhammad al-Shaybani upholding the acknowledgment based on the principle that an acknowledgment is binding and must be given effect if possible, while Abu Yusuf argues that without a specified cause the ambiguity invalidates it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and how the school resolves the conflict.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two rulings in the matn: (1) acknowledgment of a fetus (e.g., 'this fetus is for X') is valid and interpreted as a will from another; (2) acknowledgment for the benefit of a fetus itself requires a valid cause according to Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad validates it absolutely.
The first ruling is unanimous; the dispute centers on the second.
2
Identify the opposing positions
Note that Shafi'i has two narrations (one requiring specification, one not) and Malik requires certainty of existence. Also note the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
The cross-madhhab dispute adds external context but the main focus is the Hanafi internal debate.
3
Analyze Muhammad's proof
Muhammad argues that acknowledgment (iqrar) is a binding proof (hujjah mujibah) and must be applied as much as possible. Since the fetus can be owned by will or inheritance, the acknowledgment can be interpreted as a will from another, so it is valid without specifying the cause. He analogizes to the acknowledgment of an authorized slave in trade.
Key principle: possibility of validity suffices when invalidation is avoidable.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf counters that there are two possible causes (inheritance and will) with no preference, causing ambiguity that invalidates the acknowledgment. He distinguishes the authorized slave case, where only trade is the valid cause, whereas for the fetus there are two equally likely causes. He draws an analogy to an ambiguous sale scenario.
Abu Yusuf's argument rests on the concept of 'irtikab al-ashab' (committing to one of multiple possibilities) being invalid when none is specified.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi position is Muhammad's view, so an absolute acknowledgment of a fetus is valid. The text notes that if a valid cause is explicitly mentioned, there is consensus on validity; if an invalid cause is mentioned, consensus on invalidity.
The dispute is limited to the case where no cause is mentioned.

(1) ينظر : كمال الدراية (ق ٥٨٩).
And if he acknowledges regarding the fetus, it is valid, and it is interpreted as a will from someone else, and likewise for it if the confessor mentions a valid cause like inheritance and will. [2] His statement: 'And if he acknowledges...etc.' means that if a man acknowledges regarding the fetus by saying: 'the fetus of my slave-girl is for so-and-so' or 'the fetus of my sheep is for so-and-so', then it is valid because there is a way to validate it, which is a will from another, so it is interpreted accordingly, even if he does not mention the cause. Al-Shafi'i is reported by al-Muzani to have two opinions: if he is absolute, it is not valid; and another opinion that it is valid, which is the most correct, and Ahmad also said that, and Malik said: it is valid if the existence of the fetus at the time of acknowledgment is certain. Al-Samni mentioned this in 'Kamal al-Diraya'. Then if the acknowledged object is the fetus of a slave-girl, it is only entitled to the person if its existence at the time of acknowledgment is known, and that is possible if she gives birth in less than six months. Al-Ayni mentioned this in 'Sharh al-Kanz'. (3) His statement: 'And likewise for it...etc.' means that the acknowledgment for the fetus is also valid if the confessor mentions a valid cause to conceive the fetus, like inheritance and will, because he says that he should mention a valid cause related to the acknowledgment for the fetus, because the acknowledgment for the fetus is what requires the mention of a valid cause; because if he mentions a valid cause such as saying: 'his father died and he inherited from him' or 'so-and-so bequeathed it to him', it is valid, otherwise not. This is according to Abu Yusuf, and it is said that Abu Hanifa agreed with him, and Al-Shafi'i is reported to have a similar view, while according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, the acknowledgment for him is valid even if he does not mention the cause, and Al-Shafi'i in his most authentic opinion agreed, as well as Ahmad and Malik. Muhammad's reasoning in this disputed issue is that acknowledgment is a binding proof, so it must be applied as much as possible, and it is possible to interpret it as a valid cause, because it is possible that he inherited or was bequeathed to him, so invalidation is not resorted to when possible. This is like the acknowledgment of the authorized slave in trade, which is valid due to the possibility of validity, even if corruption is possible if he acknowledges with property not from trade; hence his acknowledgment regarding the shop is valid despite the possibility of corruption. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that there are two ways for its validity: inheritance and will, and neither is more likely than the other, so it becomes invalid like the case of someone who buys a slave for a thousand then sells him, and another slave with him from the seller for one thousand five hundred, then it is invalid regarding the purchased slave, even if it could be valid by making the thousand or more the share of the buyer and the rest the share of the other. This is unlike the acknowledgment of the authorized slave because its validity has only one aspect, which is trade. And unlike the acknowledgment of the fetus because its validity has only one aspect, which is a will as they said, because the fetus alone can only be owned by will, so it becomes determined as a cause. And because absolute acknowledgment is directed to acknowledgment by a cause of trade; hence the acknowledgment of the authorized slave is valid, and the acknowledgment of one of the two contracting parties against his partner is effective, and were it not for that, it would not be valid nor effective, so it becomes as if he explicitly stated it. And sale is not conceivable with a fetus, nor does anyone act as guardian for it, so it becomes invalid. The summary: there are three forms: if he mentions a cause, it is subject to the disagreement; if he mentions a valid cause, it is valid by consensus; and if he mentions an invalid cause, it is invalid by consensus. (1) See: Kamal al-Diraya (q 589).
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling of acknowledging a fetus (haml) in Hanafi fiqh. The matn states two rulings: first, if someone acknowledges a fetus (e.g., saying 'the fetus of my slave-girl belongs to X'), it is valid and is interpreted as a will (wasiyyah) from another person, meaning the fetus must have been bequeathed to X by its owner. Second, the acknowledgment for the benefit of the fetus itself is valid if the confessor mentions a valid cause (sabb salih) such as inheritance or will. The commentary then details the positions. According to the Hanafi school, the acknowledgment of a fetus is valid because it can be interpreted as a will from someone else. The commentator notes that Shafi'i has two narrations: one invalidates an absolute acknowledgment, the other validates it (the latter being more authentic). Malik requires certainty of the fetus's existence at the time of acknowledgment. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the second ruling: Abu Yusuf (and it is said Abu Hanifa) requires the confessor to mention a valid cause, while Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it valid even without mentioning the cause, as long as a valid cause is possible. Muhammad argues that acknowledgment is a binding proof (hujjah mujibah) and should be upheld if possible, comparing it to the acknowledgment of an authorized slave (madhun) in trade, where the acknowledgment is valid even if it could be corrupt. Abu Yusuf counters that there are two possible causes (inheritance and will), and neither is more likely, so the acknowledgment is ambiguous and invalid, similar to a case where a person buys a slave for 1000 and sells him, and another slave with him for 1500, which is invalid for the first slave because the price allocation is unclear. He distinguishes this from the authorized slave's acknowledgment, which has a single valid aspect (trade). For the fetus, he also argues that there is only one valid way (will), but the ambiguity between inheritance and will causes it to be invalid unless the confessor specifies. The summary is that there are three scenarios: (1) if the confessor specifies a cause, then the ruling depends on the dispute (since he might specify a valid or invalid cause); (2) if he mentions a valid cause, it is unanimously valid; (3) if he mentions an invalid cause, it is unanimously invalid.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): the fetus; unborn offspring of a slave-girl or animal
السبب الصالح (al-sabb al-salih): a valid cause that legally justifies the acknowledgment, such as inheritance or will
الإقرار حجّة موجبة (al-iqrar hujja mujiba): the principle that a person's acknowledgment is a binding proof against themselves

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, why is an absolute acknowledgment of a fetus without specifying a cause invalid?
A) Because the fetus cannot be owned except by will, and there is no way to know.
B) Because there are two possible causes (inheritance and will) and neither is more likely.
C) Because a fetus cannot be the subject of a contract.
D) Because the confessor must explicitly mention the cause in all cases.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, an absolute acknowledgment of a fetus is valid (Muhammad's view) if a valid cause is possible, while Abu Yusuf requires explicit mention of the cause.

Acknowledgment for a Fetus: Valid Causes and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining rulings on acknowledgment for a fetus with causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding acknowledgment for a fetus and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An unspecified acknowledgment for a fetus is valid and carried to a valid cause; this is the majority Hanafi view.

Acknowledgment (iqrar) is a binding unilateral declaration. When made for the benefit of a fetus, the Hanafi school requires a valid cause such as inheritance or bequest. But what if the cause is invalid or left unspecified? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, then shows why the majority position prevails.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and its rationale
Identify that acknowledgment for a fetus is valid only if a valid cause (inheritance or bequest) is mentioned. Understand why these causes are valid: the fetus can inherit and receive bequests, but cannot engage in trade or gifts.
The text specifies that a bequest to a fetus is valid and a fetus inherits.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that when the cause is invalid (sale, loan, gift) or left unspecified, there is disagreement. Abu Yusuf holds the acknowledgment void; Muhammad holds it valid and carried to a valid cause.
The phrase 'When [he] interprets as sale... or leaves unspecified, it is void' introduces Abu Yusuf's view.
3
Analyze each position's reasoning
For Abu Yusuf: a fetus cannot transact, so an invalid or unspecified cause makes the acknowledgment impossible. For Muhammad: acknowledgment is binding, so it must be enforced if possible; carrying to a valid cause is possible.
Muhammad's reasoning: 'al-iqrar hujjah mujibah' (acknowledgment is a binding proof).
4
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
The text states 'wa bihi qālati al-thalāthah' (the three agreed with him), indicating that Muhammad's view is the relied-upon position. The three likely are Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar.
The majority view thus overrides Abu Yusuf's isolated position.
5
Identify the practical difference (thamarah)
Under Abu Yusuf, the acknowledgeer pays nothing. Under the mufta bihi (Muhammad), the acknowledgeer must pay the amount to the fetus (or guardians) upon live birth.
This difference affects real cases of unspecific acknowledgments for unborn children.

فإن وَلَدَتْ حياً لأقل من نصف حول
أي ويصح الإقرار للحمل إن بين المقر سبباً صالحاً كالإرث والوصية، فإن الوصية للحمل تصح، والحمل يرث، وإن لم يبين سبباً صالحاً : كما لو بين الهية، أو قال : اشتريت له لا يصح ، وإنما لا يحتاجُ إلى ذِكْرِ السَّبب الصالح في الإقرار بالحمل ؛ لأن الوصية متعينة هناك بخلافِ الإقرار للحمل، فإنّ الأسباب متعارضة كالإرث والوصية.
(فإن وَلَدَتْ حيا لأقل من نصف حول) : أي من وقت الإقرار
(فله ما أقرّ ، وإن وَلَدَتْ حيين فلهما ، وإنْ وَلَدَتْ ميتاً فللموصي والمورث) ؛ لأنه إذا بَيْنَ السبب ، وقال : إن فلاناً أوصى بهذا للحمل، أو إن فلاناً مات وتركه ميراثاً له ، فيكون هذا إقراراً بملك الموصي ، أو المورث، فيقسم بين ورثتهما . فكذلك، وأما إذا جاءت به لأكثر من ستَّة أشهر، وهي غير معتدة لم يلزمه.
(وإن فسر ببيع ...الخ) ؛ يعني إن فسر المقر الإقرار بسبب غير صالح بأن قال : إنه باع مني هذه الدار بكذا ، أو أقرضني، أو وهب مني كذا لا يلزمه شيء، أو أبهم المقر الإقرار بلا بيان سبب أصلاً. بأن قال : علي لحمل فلانة كذا ؛ لغا ذلك الإقرار فلا يلزمه شيء أيضاً ؛ لأنه في صورة التفسير بين مستحيلاً ؛ لعدم تصوّرها من الجنين لا حقيقة، وهو ظاهر، ولا حكماً ؛ لأنه لا يولي عليه. وأما في صورة الإبهام ؛ فلأنَّ مطلق الإقرار ينصرفُ إلى الإقرار بسبب التجارة ، فيصير كما إذا صرح به، ولا يصح، فكذا هذا ، وهذا عند أبي يوسف خلافا لمحمد، فإن عنده يصح الإقرار، ويحمل على السبب الصالح، وبه قالت الثلاثة. ووجهه : إنّ الإقرار حجة موجبة، فيجب إعماله ما أمكن حمله على السبب الصالح؛ لأنه يمكن أنه ورثه أو أوصى له به ، فلا يصار إلى الإبطال مع إمكان الصحة.
